= The Semantic Enterprise: Data Architecture for the Agentic Era
Iman Poernomo
:revdate: {docdate}

== Introduction

Your data platform was built for a consumer who should no longer exist.

For thirty years, data architecture was optimized for a human analyst with SQL skills, domain knowledge, and the ability to compensate for bad infrastructure by just being smart. The databases were designed for her. The dashboards were built for or "self-served" by her. The business got the data it more or less needed thanks to the tacit understanding that she would be the one squinting at the numbers, calling a colleague when something looked off, and making a judgment about which of the three conflicting revenue figures was probably the right one. Maybe being a bit more forensic about the lineage. At best, the company may have hired a Chief Data Officer to document the names of the data elements she managed in a repository, award her the title of data steward or owner, and give her a bunch of new steercos to attend and chair.

This consumer hasn't disappeared. But, if your org is any good, the primary consumer of your data platform will soon be machine: an AI agent that queries your tables, reads your definitions, and produces answers based on the laws of context windows and attention mechanisms. Today I write in 2026: most enterprise agents are still pilots rather than production systems; the shift is underway, not complete. But it is underway fast, and if you're not thinking about the agentic consumer, you're arguably on the wrong side of the existential threat from your competition. You are building for a machine consumer who lacks tacit human intuition, who is brilliant but cost-effective only when given sufficient background, and who improvises expensively, unpredictably and confidently in the face of ambiguity. A consumer whose brain is the sum of your semantics.


[#fig-old-vs-new-consumer]
.The old consumer compensated for ambiguity; the new one amplifies it. Build for the consumer that cannot squint.
image::figures/old-vs-new-consumer.png[old vs new consumer,width=600,align="center"]

This changes how you should build your data architecture, how you govern it, what skills your team needs, what you should spend money on and what you should stop spending money on. It changes the role of the dashboard, the data catalog, the Chief Data Officer. 

Architectural patterns that have been industry standard for decades can become actively harmful. Other patterns become mandatory, ones that information architects have been arguing for since the 1990s, but largely ignored because they were consider mere hygine.

Eight convictions run through this book. That physical storage and logical meaning must be decoupled; that meaning flows in one direction and upstream always wins; that Silver inherits dimensional discipline and serves as a governed analytical surface in its own right, while Gold’s fast default is flat denormalized publication; that AI goes through the same semantic layer as every other consumer; that governance must be embedded in the execution pipeline or it does not exist; that semantic infrastructure is a prerequisite, not a feature; that the system should know what it knows and what it does not; that domain vocabularies must compose coherently — that a question spanning finance, trading, and custody must resolve to a consistent answer even though each domain defines its terms differently; and that information density per token — not context window size — is the binding constraint on every system that serves an AI consumer. None of these were deduced from first principles. They were forged in arguments, failures, and near-misses across thirteen years of running data platforms.

=== Intellectual Foundations

The formal spine of this book is decades-old mathematics that has just found its moment. Composing many domain theories into one — gluing them along what they share, the pushouts and colimits of category theory — was worked out for program synthesis in the 1990s, long before anyone needed it for data. It turns out to be exactly what the agentic age demands: a principled way to fuse a hundred local vocabularies into one coherent whole an agent can reason across, with every loss and gap made explicit rather than papered over. Here is where the ideas come from, and why they hold.

The categorical framework — domains as theories, cross-domain bridges as structure-preserving maps, and the composed enterprise view as the colimit obtained by gluing those theories along what they share — sits in a tradition of composing formal specifications by pushout. Two domains that share an entity are glued along that shared sub-theory; the result is the pushout, the smallest gluing that adds nothing the two did not already agree on; many domains compose as the colimit of the whole bridge diagram, built by iterated pushout. This is the construction I developed with Crossley and Wirsing in the late 1990s: a logical calculus whose structural rules were exactly the operations for modifying specifications — renaming, hiding, and taking unions, that last being the pushout (WADT'99; the framework later generalized across deductive systems and gathered in our 2005 monograph). Here it is transported from specifications of programs to the integration of data models — where the gluing is cheaper to state, and the hard part is no longer the static composition but its evolution. David Spivak’s functorial data migration and ologs (2012, 2014) are the recent database-specific instance of the same categorical intuition, modeling schemas as categories and migrations as functors between fixed schema maps; that work moves instances along a given map, where the concern here is composing and evolving the schemas themselves. The parent is the specification-composition tradition; Spivak is the cousin. Where this book departs from both: the colimit here is a dynamic trajectory across organizational evolution, not a static construction — an eval target with gaps, maintained by agents, not a completed mathematical object. The mathematics of the gluing was settled decades ago. What is new is operationalizing it for data platforms that change faster than any formal specification can track.

Bitemporality — valid time versus system time, the two-axis row, the three query modes — inherits from Richard Snodgrass’s foundational work on temporal databases (2000) and the SQL:2011 standard for temporal tables. The three query modes in this book (as-of-now, as-of-valid-time, as-of-report-time) correspond to Snodgrass’s current, sequenced, and nonsequenced temporal query taxonomy. Relabeling into practitioner-accessible language is deliberate, not a claim of novelty. Where this book adds: bitemporality as the default for the entire Silver substrate (not an opt-in feature for regulated workloads), and the extension to bitemporal ontology — ontologies themselves as versioned, temporal objects.

Domain-specific vocabularies connected by structure-preserving translations echo Joseph Goguen’s theory of institutions (1992) — formal systems glued together by truth-preserving maps, and the logical home of the specification-composition tradition above: the maps along which specifications are glued are institution morphisms. Morphism honesty discipline in this book is institutions made operational: not just that maps exist, but that their losses, partialities, and verification state are first-class documented objects. The bridges this book glues by pushout are exactly such maps, made to carry their own honesty data.

Dimensional modelling’s durable principles — grain discipline, conformed dimensions, slowly changing dimensions — were articulated by Ralph Kimball across multiple editions of _The Data Warehouse Toolkit_ (1996–2013). Bill Inmon’s corporate information factory established subject-oriented integration as an architectural principle. This book inherits specific principles from both traditions while claiming allegiance to neither. The synthesis — domain-canonical Silver built for columnar engines, flat Gold as strong default alongside Silver-as-Gold views for dimensional depth, semantic layer as the logical ontology governing both — is its own position, shaped by constraints that did not exist when either tradition was formalized.

Zhamak Dehghani’s data mesh (2022) correctly identified domain ownership as a structural principle and centralized data teams as bottlenecks. This book’s critique — that mesh underspecifies the semantic layer, producing federation without composition — is a critique of implementation gaps, not of the ownership principle. Federated governance as practiced here (central semantic layer, domain-owned Gold, explicit morphisms) owes more to mesh than it argues against.

One intellectual decision requires explicit defense. This book prescribes prose documentation (domain essays of 600–1500 words) over structured knowledge representation (OWL ontologies, SPARQL endpoints, formal description logics). The Semantic Web program — OWL, RDF, linked data — was explicitly built for machine reasoning. Dismissing it deserves an argument, not an assumption.

The argument: the dominant machine consumer of enterprise data documentation has shifted from symbolic reasoners (which process OWL) to large language models (which process natural language). OWL supports automated consistency checking and subsumption reasoning. Prose supports contextual understanding of edge cases, conventions, and operational craft. Both are machine-readable. They serve different reasoning architectures. When the primary machine consumer was a reasoner checking class hierarchies, OWL was correct. When the primary machine consumer is an LLM reasoning over operational context, prose is correct. This is not a regression from structured knowledge representation. It is a format shift driven by a consumer shift — the same argument the book makes about every other architectural decision.

Structured metadata anchors (datatypes, keys, refresh cadence, lineage) that survive from the ISO 11179 tradition serve the cross-reference and integration functions that structured formats handle well. Semantic weight — the operational knowledge that determines whether an agent reasons correctly or plausibly — lives in the essay. Both formats, serving different functions, governed together.

Examples use dbt, Looker, BigQuery, and Claude because they are drawn from practice. The architecture is stack-agnostic —- every principle works with whatever tools your organization has committed to. The final essay is prescriptive: what to do Monday morning. The rest earn that right by making the argument first.

The assumed reader runs a data platform, leads a data engineering team, or is responsible for the analytical infrastructure of an organization. Familiarity with semantic layers, dbt, and the Bronze/Silver/Gold convention is assumed. Agreement is not. Every opposing position is stated fairly, then dismantled. You will recognize yourself in at least one of them.

If this book does its job, you will finish it with a different mental model of what a data platform is for: an infrastructure for making facts available to any consumer —- human, dashboard, AI agent, regulatory submission —- through a shared vocabulary that makes the facts mean the same thing to everyone who touches them.

Language —- human language —- is what data architecture is made of. Your column names, your definitions, your glossary terms, your schema descriptions: these are not documentation. They are the interface between your organization’s knowledge and every system that reasons over it. Get them right, and your AI agents reason correctly, your dashboards agree with each other, and your new hires understand the data estate in their first week. Get them wrong, and no amount of dashboard redesign or governance committee meetings will save you.

Not a comfortable position for an industry that thinks of itself as building pipelines. Pipelines are plumbing. The craft —- the actual hard work, the thing that determines whether your platform creates clarity or confusion —- is deciding what "revenue" means, where the boundary between "trading" and "market-making" falls, whether "position" is one concept or three. Linguistic decisions with engineering consequences, and the industry has systematically underinvested in the people and tools needed to make them well.

AI changes the calculation. Not by making the decisions easier —- they are as hard as they have ever been —- but by making the consequences of getting them wrong impossible to ignore, and the infrastructure to get them right dramatically cheaper to build.

== The Shift

Actually, data platforms fail all the time. They don't fail when you've tracking clean metadata. This has always been the case. But AI is a forcing function. A crap data platform with patchy semantics can still be usable if you've got humans willing to patch together gaps in understanding, remember where things are, keep a record of the weird tweaks and nuances of where to find data and what it means. A crap data platform with patchy semantics means enterprise agentic intelligence is a non starter. 

=== The 1% Problem

A team was showing off an MCP-to-database solution that allows a user to query and analyse data on their lake. Not a vendor demo with canned data —- real production data, real queries, real business questions asked by real executives who had not been prepped on what to ask. The kind of demo where the CEO asks something nobody anticipated. The system was running at 98 to 99 percent accuracy on ad hoc queries. Ninety-eight to ninety-nine percent. On arbitrary natural language questions against a production data warehouse, with no canned responses, no tricks. Pretty good: that is a number that makes people lean forward in their chairs and start imagining what their organization would look like with this capability. Demo with Exco went well and a go decision was made to roll out to the organization.

But what mattered to be prime time was the 1 to 2 percent failure rate — not its size, but its cause. Every single failure traced to the same root cause. The model was smart enough. Every failure was a context failure. The AI did not know what a term meant because nobody had told it — not because the term was obscure or the concept complex, but because nobody had written a definition and put it where the AI could read it.

One query failed because an internal metric —- a score the research team used daily, discussed in meetings, referenced in reports —- was not mapped in the semantic layer. The analyst who built the dashboard called it one thing. The warehouse table used a completely different name. No one had built the mapping. The AI either returned nothing or guessed. Both outcomes are bad: returning nothing makes the stakeholder think the data does not exist; guessing makes the stakeholder think the data says something it does not.

Another query returned zeros for collateral values —- every row present and correct in the warehouse, but the table carried a machine-internal name that matched nothing a stakeholder would ask for. The AI could not find what it could not name, and reported "no data" while the data sat untouched. The fix took minutes: map the business concept to the physical table in the semantic layer.

In every case, the fix was the same: add the definition to the semantic layer. Map the business concept to the physical table. Tell the AI what the term means, in language it can process, and the query works.

This pattern repeated so consistently that the team developed a rule of thumb: when the AI gets it wrong, check the semantic layer first. Nine times out of ten, the definition is missing or the name does not match the concept. The tenth time, the definition exists but is ambiguous enough that the AI misinterpreted it —- which is the same problem at a different level of granularity.

Semantic infrastructure is a bottleneck to agentic analysis. Billions go to on larger models, better architectures, more sophisticated training regimes. All of that matters. But for enterprise data —- for whether an AI agent can answer your CFO’s question correctly —- the constraint is not the model. The constraint is whether someone wrote a three-sentence description of what "trading volume" means and put it somewhere the AI can read it. The glamorous constraint is the model. The actual constraint is the vocabulary.

Benchmarks point the same way, though the numbers need reading with care, because they come from different measurement regimes. On adversarial enterprise benchmarks scored by strict execution accuracy —- does the generated query return exactly the right rows —- raw text-to-SQL against unstructured schemas is genuinely hard: reported accuracies run from the low tens of percent upward, and on the hardest suites frontier models score worse still. Those scores understate real-world performance, because strict execution scoring also penalizes answers a human would accept. Pointed the other way, recent vendor testing on a well-named, well-modeled flat schema reports practical accuracy in the mid-nineties with no semantic layer at all —- which is the same lesson from the other side: the data model carries the meaning. The claim is therefore not a single leap from 40 percent to 100. It is this: ambiguity in the schema is what produces wrong answers, and a governed semantic layer removes that ambiguity by construction. Where a query falls within the semantic layer’s scope, the LLM selects metrics and dimensions and the query engine generates the SQL deterministically, so the model cannot produce a subtly wrong result —- accuracy on that query class approaches the ceiling. The model is the same model. The infrastructure changed. The win is  against ambiguity, not against the model’s intelligence.

This should keep every data leader up at night. AI consumes more than your data. It reads your standards, your architecture documents, your naming conventions, your glossary. Every piece of written material that describes what your data means is now read by machines. If those documents are ambiguous —- and most of them are, because they were written for humans who fill in gaps with context —- your AI will inherit your confusion and operationalize it at scale. The machine reader does not compensate. It reproduces.

=== The Binding Constraint

Most conversations about AI and data start in the wrong place — with context windows. How many tokens can the model hold? How much schema can we fit? The assumption is that the problem is volume —- that if we could just get enough data into the prompt, the agent would figure it out. This assumption is wrong, and it is wrong in a way that has direct architectural consequences.

What matters is not how much context fits in the window but how much meaning each token carries. Call it information density per token. A context window stuffed with ten thousand tokens of raw DDL and sample rows is not ten times more useful than a thousand tokens of governed semantic definitions. It is frequently less useful, because every token of noise dilutes every token of signal, and attention does not scale linearly with context length. The model does not process its hundredth token with the same fidelity it applies to its tenth. Long-context degradation in recall and reasoning accuracy is well-attested in the research literature on frontier models. More context is not better context. Denser context is better context.

This reframes the entire architectural question. The goal is not "get data to the agent." The goal is "maximize the information density of what reaches the agent." These are different engineering objectives, and they produce different architectures.

==== Two scenarios on a trading desk

Consider an equity trading desk with the usual core data assets: orders, executions, positions, counterparty records, instrument references, desk identifiers. The data lives in silver and gold layers on a modern lakehouse stack. An analyst —- or, increasingly, an agent acting on behalf of one —- asks a question that sounds straightforward:

"What was our desk’s P&L by strategy last quarter?"

Well-posed in English. Not well-posed in SQL until at least four embedded ambiguities are resolved. Which P&L —- realized, unrealized, mark-to-market, or a desk-specific definition that includes funding cost? What counts as "strategy" —- is that the strategy tag on the order, the strategy classifier on the execution, or the portfolio-level strategy assignment? "Last quarter" in which calendar —- strict calendar quarter, fiscal quarter, or trading-day quarter? And which desk —- the organizational desk, the booking entity, or the risk desk?

A competent human analyst on the desk resolves these in seconds, often without consciously enumerating them, because every answer is embedded in tribal knowledge. "We use mark-to-market unless the question is for the compensation committee." "Strategy means the portfolio tag, not the order tag —- the order tag is unreliable since the migration." "Last quarter means calendar Q1 unless Finance sent the email." An agent has no tribal knowledge. The agent has whatever you put in its context. Everything that follows is about that.

*Scenario A.* The agent receives the six relevant tables as DDL —- CREATE TABLE statements —- plus five sample rows from each. A condensed version of what arrives: column names like `exec_id`, `order_id`, `cpty_id`, `cpty_tier`, `instrument_id`, `desk_id`, `exec_ts`, `quantity`, `price`, `side`, `ingestion_ts`. The counterparties table has `cpty_id`, `cpty_name`, `cpty_tier`, `valid_from`, `valid_to`, `is_current`. The instruments table has `instrument_id`, `isin`, `ticker`, `region_code`, `asset_class`. And so on for orders, positions, desks. The full payload runs roughly twenty-five hundred tokens.

Walk through what the agent can determine from this. It can guess that `strategy_id` on the orders table represents some kind of strategy classification. It cannot tell whether the strategy tag on executions denotes the strategy at order time, the strategy at fill time, or a downstream assignment that was applied after the fact. Two columns with similar names on different tables —- the agent assumes they are equivalent. They probably are not. It can see `valid_from`, `valid_to`, and `is_current` columns on the counterparties dimension and may correctly infer SCD2. It will not know which column on executions is the surrogate for strategy-at-time, because the schema does not say. It has no P&L definition. There are price and quantity columns; the agent can construct a notional, but mark-to-market versus realized versus unrealized is not derivable from the schema, and no documentation flags the absence.

A plausible SQL query results. It joins six tables. It picks the order-level strategy tag because it appears first in the schema and has a simpler join path —- a reasonable heuristic that happens to select the deprecated classifier. It conflates two temporal grains: the strategy at order time and the strategy at fill time, treating them as interchangeable because nothing in the DDL distinguishes them. It computes a notional P&L from price times quantity, which is neither mark-to-market nor realized nor anything the desk would recognize as a number they manage to. It applies calendar-quarter logic to a desk that runs on fiscal quarters, because nothing in the schema says otherwise and calendar quarters are the default assumption.

Numbers come back. Wrong in four independent ways, any one of which would make the answer useless for decision-making. But the numbers look plausible —- they are in the right order of magnitude, they break down by something that looks like a strategy, they cover something that looks like a quarter. A busy desk head glancing at the output might not catch the errors for hours, or days, or until a downstream decision has already been made on the basis of numbers that were fabricated from structural ambiguity. The agent reports them with confidence, because the schema gave it no reason to qualify. This is the failure mode that matters. The failure is not that the agent declines to answer. The failure is that the agent answers wrongly and presents the answer as authoritative.

*Scenario B.* The agent receives a different context. Three components.

First, a six-hundred-word domain essay on the equity trading data substrate, covering provenance, the calendar convention (calendar quarter unless explicitly fiscal), the strategy classification hierarchy (portfolio-level is canonical, order-level is deprecated since the migration), the three canonical P&L definitions and which is the desk default, and the temporal contracts on executions and counterparties.

Second, a semantic-layer declaration exposing the executions fact and the strategy dimension with grains, joins, and measures declared explicitly. The measures name `pnl_mtm` as mark-to-market P&L with a description: "Default measure for strategy analysis." A second measure, `pnl_realised`, carries the note: "Use when explicitly requested." The join to the strategy dimension specifies the relationship, the join key, and a human-readable description: "Portfolio-level strategy assignment. Default for desk-level analysis. Do not use order-level strategy tag."

Third, per-table temporal contract metadata indicating that executions are bitemporal at row level, that the strategy dimension is SCD2 with portfolio-level assignment exposed as the default, and that "last quarter" resolves to calendar Q1 unless the query specifies fiscal.

Roughly eighteen hundred tokens total. Less than the schema dump.

The agent now reads, before it reasons, that the default P&L is mark-to-market, that the strategy classifier is the portfolio tag, that calendar quarter applies, and that desk means the organizational desk with a specific join path. It composes the query. It uses the declared measure. It joins through the declared relationship. It applies the documented calendar convention. The query is correct. The answer is correct. Where it chose a default, it surfaced the choice rather than burying it. Where it could not resolve an ambiguity from the documentation, it flagged the gap rather than guessing.

Less context. More fidelity.

Same model. What changed is what the tokens carried. In Scenario A, twenty-five hundred tokens delivered column names, datatypes, and sample values —- raw structural information that is necessary for query generation but radically insufficient for query correctness. In Scenario B, eighteen hundred tokens delivered declared measures, named relationships, documented conventions, and explicit warnings about deprecated fields. Seven hundred fewer tokens. Dramatically more meaning per token. The difference between wrong and right was better context, not more of it.

[#fig-context-per-token]
.Context per Token: Scenario A versus Scenario B
image::figures/context-per-token.png[Context per Token: Scenario A versus Scenario B,width=680,align=center]


This pattern is structural, not specific to trading desks. Any domain with institutional conventions —- which is every domain —- will produce the same divergence between schema-dump and semantic-layer approaches. The schema tells the agent what the data looks like. The semantic layer tells the agent what the data means. Confusing the two is like handing someone a dictionary and expecting them to write a novel in a language they have never spoken.

==== Why this is the constraint that governs everything else

Marginal tokens in the schema-dump scenario are mostly redundant. Each additional sample row reveals patterns already visible from the first three. The marginal token in the semantic-layer-plus-essay scenario carries genuinely new structural information —- a relational fact, a documented edge case, a temporal contract surfaced explicitly. Doubling the schema dump does not approach the fidelity of the semantic approach, because the bottleneck is not how many sample rows the agent sees. The bottleneck is what the rows mean, and that information is not in the rows.

Three reasons this is the binding constraint for the entire architecture.

Context windows expand but never become free. Every additional token costs latency, inference cost, and —- more subtly —- attention dilution. A system whose architecture requires fifty thousand tokens of context to answer a single business question correctly is, at sufficient query volume, operationally indistinguishable from a system that does not work. The fantasy of infinite context windows —- the idea that we will eventually just dump the whole schema and let the model sort it out —- ignores that attention is a finite resource even when tokens are cheap. A 200,000-token context window with 180,000 tokens of noise and 20,000 tokens of signal will underperform a 5,000-token window where every token carries structural meaning. The architecture that stuffs the window is betting against the physics of attention. The architecture that curates the window is working with it.

The question for agentic data systems is never whether a particular agent answers a particular query in a particular happy path. It is whether the system generalizes across domains the agent has not seen before. A schema-dump approach can be hand-tuned for a narrow set of well-rehearsed query patterns. It collapses the moment composition across unfamiliar domains is required. A semantic-layer-plus-essay approach degrades gracefully because each domain’s contribution to the agent’s context is high in relational density and low in redundancy. The system stays answerable as the surface area grows. This is the difference between a demo and a product.

The constraint is uniquely exposed by agentic workloads. Human analysts handle low information density per token by importing their own context —- domain knowledge, organizational lore, the muscle memory of a thousand previous queries. The deficit of an undocumented schema is absorbed silently by the analyst’s reservoir of background knowledge. Agents have no such reservoir. Whatever is not in the context is not being reasoned over. The deficit does not degrade gracefully. It becomes failure. This is what makes the agent the most honest consumer your data platform has ever had: it cannot fake understanding. It either has the context or it does not, and when it does not, the failure is visible immediately rather than buried in an analyst’s silent compensation.

Context cost is measured in reasoning quality per token, not in tokens. A thousand tokens of governed semantic definitions that produce a correct answer cost less —- in every dimension that matters —- than ten thousand tokens of raw DDL that produce a wrong one. The inference bill is lower. The latency is lower. The error rate is lower. The trust is higher. The economics run in one direction, and they run hard.

For AI-aware data architecture, the binding constraint is not where data sits, how fast queries return, or how cheaply it is stored. It is the rate at which the system delivers structurally meaningful, compositionally usable signal into a bounded context window. Context per token. This is the concept that governs everything else in this essay and, if the argument holds, everything else in the way you build your data platform from this point forward. Every architectural decision —- how you model your tables, how you document your domains, how you expose your data to consumers —- is either increasing or decreasing the information density per token that an agent receives. The organizations that optimize for this metric will build platforms that work. The organizations that optimize for context volume will build platforms that produce confident, articulate, wrong answers.

==== Toward a metric

A fair objection: "context per token" as used above is a practitioner heuristic, not a formal metric. The critic who calls it unfalsifiable in its current form is correct. Without a precise definition, any success can be attributed to high density and any failure to low density, which is tautology, not measurement. The concept deserves more rigor than it has received so far.

What we mean precisely: context per token is the ratio of decision-relevant semantic content to total token count in an agent’s context window. A token is decision-relevant if removing it would change the agent’s query plan or degrade the correctness of its answer. A token is noise if removing it would not. This definition is clear enough to reason about. It is not yet clear enough to compute.

Three candidate formalizations point toward what a proper metric would look like.

First, mutual information. Measure the mutual information between the context provided and the probability distribution over correct SQL queries. Higher mutual information means higher density — the context reduces uncertainty about the correct query by a greater amount per token consumed. This has the advantage of being grounded in information theory and the disadvantage of requiring a ground-truth distribution over correct queries, which is expensive to construct.

Second, task-specific accuracy normalized by token count. For a fixed set of business questions, measure accuracy and divide by the number of context tokens consumed. Accuracy-per-token gives a comparable ratio across context strategies: schema dump versus semantic layer, verbose versus compressed, flat versus joined. The metric is simple. It conflates two axes — accuracy and efficiency — in a single number, which is both its appeal and its limitation.

Third, compression ratio. Take N tokens of raw schema and determine how many tokens of semantic context produce equivalent answer quality. The ratio is the density multiplier. If 1,800 tokens of semantic context match the accuracy of 25,000 tokens of raw DDL, the multiplier is roughly 14x. This is the most intuitive of the three and the easiest to compute empirically. It is also the least theoretically grounded, because "equivalent answer quality" requires its own operationalization.

None of these exists as a standardized benchmark. No suite measures information density per token across data platforms. No leaderboard ranks context strategies by semantic efficiency. The concept is defended today by repeated observation — Scenario A versus Scenario B, every time, across every domain we have tested — not by measurement against an accepted scale.

The claim is, however, falsifiable in principle. The experiment is straightforward. Take one hundred business questions across ten domains. For each, prepare two contexts: a schema dump and a semantic-layer-plus-essay. Measure accuracy, token count, and latency. If semantic contexts do not consistently produce higher accuracy at lower token counts, the claim is wrong. The experiment has not been run at scale. It should be.

Context per token is the heuristic this architecture optimizes for. It awaits its benchmark. The authors expect it to survive measurement, but the measurement has not been done.

=== The Strategies That Fail

Four positions worth arguing against, because at least one is probably your current strategy. Each of them is wrong.

==== "AI is just another BI consumer"

This is the most common position and the most dangerous. The reasoning goes: we already serve dashboards, reports, analysts, and data scientists. AI is just one more consumer. Give it SQL access, point it at the schema, let it figure out the table structures and column names the same way a new analyst would. Same data, different interface.

The reasoning is elegant. The reasoning is wrong.

Dashboards tolerate ambiguity because humans compensate. When a dashboard shows two different revenue numbers on two different pages, a human calls colleagues, discovers one includes intercompany transfers and the other excludes them, and makes a judgment about which definition matches her question. The ambiguity existed. The human resolved it.

An AI encounters the same ambiguity and does something qualitatively different. It does not call anyone. It picks one of the two numbers based on whatever heuristic its training data suggests and returns it with four decimal places, a grammatically perfect explanation, and no indication that another number exists or that the choice was arbitrary.

Eighty-four percent of data teams regularly encounter conflicting versions of the same metric. This is the normal state of enterprise data, not a niche problem at a few disorganized companies. Multiple definitions of the same concept, living in different systems, maintained by different teams, diverging over time. Humans navigate this by building relationships and institutional knowledge. AI navigates this by picking one and being wrong half the time.

Vendor testing shows the same pattern. Google’s internal testing found that their semantic modeling layer reduces data errors in AI natural language queries by as much as two-thirds. The gain comes not from a smarter model but from a semantic layer that removes the ambiguity behind the errors.

Treating AI as just another BI consumer means deploying it into an environment that was designed for consumers who compensate for ambiguity. AI does not compensate. AI amplifies. Every ambiguity in your data estate —- every conflicting definition, every undocumented calculation, every column named with a machine-internal identifier —- becomes a potential hallucination when an AI agent encounters it. The dashboard could tolerate the mess because the human using it could not. The AI agent inherits the mess and presents it as truth.

==== "Let us just add a chatbot to our dashboards"

This pitch appears in boardrooms with predictable regularity. The pitch is seductive: take your existing BI tool, add a natural language interface, and now anyone can query the data without learning SQL or navigating a complicated dashboard. Conversational analytics in ninety days. The vendor promises it. The demo looks great. The executive sponsor signs the check.

A chatbot that queries bad data through a bad semantic layer is not conversational analytics. It is automated confusion. It is your existing mess, delivered faster, to more people, with less oversight.


[#fig-chatbot-confusion]
.A chatbot on ungoverned data is not conversational analytics — it is automated confusion, delivered faster, to more people.
image::figures/chatbot-confusion.png[chatbot confusion,width=600,align="center"]

Sixty-seven percent of enterprise chatbot deployments failed to meet expectations. Only 6 percent of IT leaders believe their chatbot technology is effective and highly adopted. An airline’s customer service chatbot was held legally liable for giving a passenger incorrect information about bereavement fares —- the chatbot invented a refund policy that did not exist, stated it with perfect confidence, and a tribunal ruled the airline was bound by what its agent had promised. An automotive company’s chatbot agreed to sell a vehicle for one dollar after a customer exploited a prompt injection vulnerability. A fintech company replaced 700 human agents with AI, then watched customer satisfaction drop and complaints rise until they had to reverse course and hire humans back.

These are not edge cases or the work of incompetent teams. They are predictable failures that share a common architecture: an AI system deployed without semantic infrastructure, without governed definitions, without a layer that tells the model what is true, what is uncertain, and what it should refuse to answer. The chatbot was confident because no one built the guardrails that would have made it appropriately uncertain. Epistemic honesty —- the quality of knowing what you know and what you do not —- is a feature of the infrastructure, not of the model. If the infrastructure does not encode uncertainty, the model will not express it.

The fix was never a chatbot. The fix was promoting derived calculations to governed tables with explicit definitions, catalogued in the semantic layer, accessible to both humans and machines. The knowledge has to live in the infrastructure, not in one person’s head. This is a knowledge management problem that AI makes impossible to ignore, not an AI problem.

==== "Data mesh will solve the governance problem"

Data mesh got something genuinely important right.

Data mesh correctly identified that centralized data teams cannot understand every domain. Domain experts should own their data products. This insight is correct and valuable.

What data mesh underspecified is the semantic layer. Domain ownership without a shared vocabulary means each domain defines terms independently. Trading defines "volume" one way. Finance defines it another. Research uses a third definition. Each is reasonable within its domain. Each is documented —- if the organization is lucky —- in its domain’s data product specification. And when an AI agent queries across domains —- which is what executives do, because executives do not think in domain boundaries, they think in questions like "what is our total exposure?" that span trading, finance, and risk —- it encounters the same concept under three different names with three different calculation methods.

This is exactly the problem that sank the 98-99 percent demo described earlier, scaled to the organizational level. The AI cannot bridge gaps that nobody has mapped. Federation without semantic unification is distributed confusion. You need both: domain ownership and a shared vocabulary that makes cross-domain queries coherent.

The industry is converging on this recognition. Major technology companies that adopted mesh principles early are now moving to what they publicly describe as "balanced, federated approaches" that formalize domain ownership while embedding governance, quality, and interoperability directly into shared platforms. The key word is interoperability, and interoperability requires a shared semantic layer that all domains contribute to and all consumers —- including AI agents —- can query against.

==== "We'll just hardcode the orchestration against our APIs and treat the model like a closed system"

This is the currently fashionable safe play.

Build a lightweight orchestration layer (MCP, or whatever the acronym of the month is). Hardcode the important paths against your existing APIs. Keep the model on a tight leash. It is contained. It is auditable. It does not require touching the rest of the platform.

It is safe. It is also small-minded. It treats the model like a slightly smarter intern who still needs you to draw the entire map for it every single time. The upside is capped at whatever you had the foresight, and the political will, to hardcode in advance.

The even worse version currently making the rounds: "just give the agent broad access and let it crawl and self-discover." This is not architecture. This is hoping enlightenment will magically emerge from effluent. The agent will confidently traverse the worst parts of your data estate, invent plausible-sounding joins, and present the result as insight. It will be wrong with maximum confidence, and you will have built exactly zero infrastructure to stop it.

Both positions share the same root failure: they treat the model as the variable and the data estate as fixed. The correct position is the opposite.

==== "AI changes nothing fundamental — it is still just garbage in, garbage out. Focus on data quality."

This is the laziest position currently enjoying serious airtime from CDOs and data leaders.

It is technically true in the same trivial sense that "breathing is important for life" is technically true. It is also completely useless as a position, because the people saying it have *zero solutions* for what "good data" actually looks like when the consumer is no longer a human who can squint, call a colleague, and paper over the gaps.

Any intelligence — human, postman, or frontier model — requires *brain and memory*. Data is not the raw material that intelligence acts upon after the fact. In an enterprise, data *is* the deep thought. It is the precondition of thought itself.

=== The Coincidence That Is Not a Coincidence

AI demands: clean definitions, canonical tables, governed vocabularies, machine-readable metadata, explicit relationships between concepts, default assumptions documented in structured formats, navigational heuristics that guide queries to the right data. This is a lot. This is the kind of infrastructure that has been on every Chief Data Officer’s roadmap for the last decade, perpetually deprioritized because the cost was high and the benefit was diffuse. Under the old regime —- where the primary consumer was a human analyst who could compensate for ambiguity —- most organizations correctly concluded that the cost of building all of this was not justified by the benefit. The analyst could squint. The business ran fine. Semantic infrastructure was a nice-to-have that never survived the quarterly prioritization meeting because there was always a more urgent dashboard to build, a more pressing pipeline to fix, a more visible executive request to fulfill.

AI changes the economics of this calculation. And it changes them in both directions simultaneously.

On the demand side, AI makes semantic infrastructure mandatory. Without it, your AI projects fail. Sixty percent of them, according to Gartner. The cost of not having semantic infrastructure is no longer "analysts are slightly less efficient." The cost is "your AI strategy does not work." That changes the prioritization calculus dramatically. When semantic infrastructure was competing against "build the finance dashboard," it lost. When semantic infrastructure is competing against "our $10 million AI investment produces wrong answers," it wins.

On the supply side, AI makes semantic infrastructure cheap to build.

Consider what it used to take. At institutions where building a semantic layer for a single domain was a multi-year program, the pattern was always the same: dedicated teams, months of workshops, taxonomy design, ontology construction, data dictionary compilation, quarterly governance reviews. Eighteen months to two years from inception to delivery.

Formal ontology projects are the extreme version of this pattern —- tens of thousands of terms, years of work by specialists, exquisitely rigorous, formally beautiful. Industry adoption: negligible — not because they are wrong, but because they are documents, not living systems. And documents decay at the speed of organizational change, which is always faster than the speed of documentation maintenance.

Now consider what AI makes possible. Recording a conversation with a domain expert and using AI to extract, structure, and validate definitions in a single session —- producing results more complete and consistent than six months of traditional documentation. Running automated quality checks against international standards on every commit, not quarterly. Monitoring the entire data estate for drift —- new tables without definitions, renamed columns, changed calculations —- through continuous automated surveillance rather than manual audits that happen twice a year if someone remembers.

Glossary terms across multiple domains, with logical concepts mapped, quality-scored, and embedded in the execution pipeline —- built in months rather than years. A productivity improvement is doing the same thing 20 percent faster. This is a phase transition: the thing that was too expensive to do properly is now cheap enough to do continuously.

Maintenance was always the bottleneck. Building a glossary was hard but achievable. Maintaining it as the data changed underneath it —- that was the part that killed every previous attempt. The graveyard of abandoned metadata catalogs in every large organization tells the story: well-intentioned, well-funded initiatives that produced beautiful artifacts and then watched them decay because maintenance is unglamorous, expensive, and never-ending. AI solves the maintenance problem.

Information architects have been arguing for governed vocabularies for decades. They were right. They were also right to be ignored, because the business case never closed. The cost of building and maintaining semantic infrastructure exceeded the cost of letting humans compensate for its absence. That was a rational calculation. It is no longer rational. AI broke it in both directions: the cost of absence went up, and the cost of building went down. The lines have crossed.

The industry is converging on this recognition. In 2025, major data platform vendors joined forces to create the Open Semantic Interchange standard. When competitors collaborate on a standard, the architecture is settling. The debate about whether you need a semantic layer is over. The remaining debates are implementation debates.

Gartner projects that by 2027, organizations that prioritize semantics in their AI-ready data strategies will increase model accuracy by up to 80 percent and reduce costs by up to 60 percent. By 2028, 60 percent of agentic analytics projects without a consistent semantic layer will fail. The early adopters who skipped the semantic layer are already dealing with the consequences: hallucinated joins, conflicting metrics, confident wrong answers, and the slow realization that you cannot shortcut the foundation.

=== The New Primary Consumer

When your team designs a new data model —- a new Gold table, a new analytical dataset, a new metric —- who are they designing it for?

If the answer is "the Tableau dashboard that the finance team requested," you are optimizing for the wrong consumer.

The finance team’s needs matter. But the design process that starts with "what does this dashboard need?" produces a fundamentally different architecture than the design process that starts with "what does this concept mean, and how should any consumer —- human or machine —- access it?"

Dashboard-centric design produces tables shaped for a specific visual, with columns named for the chart they appear in and business logic scattered across SQL, visualization tool calculated fields, and the analyst’s mental model. The result works for the analyst who built it. It is opaque to everyone else.

Semantic-centric design produces tables shaped for the concept they represent, with columns named for what they mean in the business domain and canonical business logic embedded in the governed model. Definitions explicit, relationships documented, assumptions stated. The result works for the analyst, the next analyst, the AI agent, and the new hire who needs to understand the data estate without calling twelve people.

Teams denormalize data into an analytical layer for fast access. They celebrate the performance gains. The queries are fast, the stakeholders are happy, the team moves on to the next project. Nobody catalogs what they have built. There is no time. The next project is already overdue. More tables get added to the analytical layer. Each one answers a specific question for a specific stakeholder, and each one has its own definitions, its own calculations, its own interpretation of concepts like "revenue" and "active customer." Replication happens without governance —- the same data appears in multiple tables with slightly different transformations, and nobody tracks which version is canonical.

Within two years, the analytical layer is a swamp. Hundreds of tables, nobody knows what half of them contain, nobody trusts the numbers. Any organization that denormalizes without cataloging will produce this swamp. There are no exceptions. The only variable is how long it takes people to admit it.

Architecture prevents this, not policy and not a governance committee that meets quarterly to review proposed changes. When every analytical table ships with a governed definition —- when the infrastructure will not let you publish a table without describing what it contains, what it means, and how it should be used —- cataloging becomes mandatory rather than optional. You cannot skip it because the system will not let you skip it. The discipline of building the semantic interface compels the discipline of governing the data. Architecture prevents the failure that process could not.

Every join is a decision the machine can get wrong. Pick the wrong key, assume the wrong cardinality, apply the wrong type — each one a place where a five-table query quietly returns a confident, wrong number. Joins are hallucination vectors. A star schema does not just ask the AI to retrieve your data; it asks the AI to reconstruct your data model on every query, and to be right about it every time. It will not be.

This is not a style preference. It has a price, and the price is measurable. A five-table star schema costs three to five times more input tokens and two to three times more output tokens than a single flat table carrying its own semantic definition — more tokens to describe the schema, more to reason about the join paths, more to generate the multi-table SQL, and a fresh chance to get it wrong at each step. Snowflake’s AI SQL research found naive query plans triggering over 110,000 model calls where optimized, join-free plans achieved a 300x reduction. Three hundred times. That is the gap between an architecture that fights the machine and one that feeds it.


[#fig-tax-300x]
.The 300× tax: a physical star schema makes the agent reason through joins on every query. Flatten Gold and the tax disappears.
image::figures/tax-300x.png[tax 300x,width=640,align="center"]

So flatten Gold. Denormalize to the question — one wide row, every column defined once in the semantic layer, no joins for the consumer to fumble. The same architecture that stops the AI hallucinating also stops your analysts squinting and your new hires guessing which of forty revenue tables is the real one. There is one definition, not forty. This is not machine readability bought at the cost of human usability; it is both, at once, for the first time.

And here is the part the dimensional-modelling veterans need to hear, because it is the part everyone gets wrong when they hear “flatten”: dimensional structure is not eliminated. It is relocated. Grain discipline, conformed dimensions, SCD2 — all of it lives on, in Silver, where it belongs. When an analyst genuinely needs to drill and slice across dimensions, the semantic layer exposes Silver’s dimensional structure directly: star-schema-equivalent analytical power, with no physical star schema in Gold for any consumer — human or machine — to trip over. You keep the discipline. You kill the joins. The thing dimensional modelling was for survives; the thing that made it expensive for AI does not. If you are still building physical star schemas at the consumption layer, you are paying a 300x tax to make your AI dumber.

=== Five Changes to Make This Week

If the shift is real, the question is what to do about it. Here is what works.

First, change who you design for. The next time your team builds a Gold table or an analytical model, the design review should include a question that nobody is currently asking: "Can an AI agent query this table and get the correct answer on the first try, without any additional context beyond what is in the semantic layer?" If the answer is no —- if the column names are opaque, if the definitions are missing, if the business logic is documented only in someone’s head or embedded in a visualization tool’s calculated field —- the model is not finished. It does not matter how well it performs in a dashboard. If a machine cannot reason about it, it is incomplete. This is a quality standard, not a feature request.

Second, audit your vocabulary. How many definitions of "revenue" exist in your organization? If you do not know the answer, that is the answer. Start with your top twenty business concepts. Define them in a structured, versioned, machine-readable artifact that lives in your deployment pipeline —- not in a PDF or a Confluence page that will be out of date in six months. Get business stakeholders to sign off, because if the business does not own the vocabulary, nobody trusts the vocabulary.

With AI-assisted definition extraction, a working vocabulary for a critical domain takes days, not months.

Third, stop treating the semantic layer as optional infrastructure. If your AI strategy is "connect the chatbot to the database and let it write SQL," you are building Air Canada’s chatbot. The semantic layer is the governance mechanism that stands between your AI and your data. AI queries the semantic layer, not the database. This is the single most important design principle in any data architecture built after 2024. That layer knows what the terms mean, how the calculations work, and —- critically —- what the AI should refuse to answer because the data is stale, incomplete, or outside governed scope.

Fourth, address the token economics. If you are still building star schemas for your analytical layer, you are burning money. Flat, denormalized tables with semantic definitions are cheaper to query, faster to process, and dramatically less error-prone. This is not a marginal optimization. The research shows a 300x difference in AI call volume between join-heavy and join-free architectures. Design for flat consumption as the default. Your AI budget will thank you. Your accuracy metrics will thank you more.

Fifth, address the organizational reality with candour and empathy. What is described in this essay threatens some people’s professional identity. Data teams built their careers on star schemas, on dimensional modeling, on dashboard design, on the craft of making data visually comprehensible. Telling them "your primary consumer is now a machine" sounds like telling them their skills are obsolete.

Their skills are not obsolete. The analyst who deeply understands what "revenue" means is more important than ever —- she is just expressing that understanding through a governed definition rather than a calculated field in a visualization tool that only she can maintain. The engineer who names columns meaningfully is more valuable than ever, because column names are the primary interface between data and AI agents.

The shift is not from human to machine. It is from implicit to explicit. From knowledge that lives in people’s heads to knowledge that lives in the infrastructure. The craftsperson who refuses to learn the new medium will find themselves maintaining dashboards that fewer and fewer people use, while the organization’s questions are answered by AI agents that query the semantic layer they did not help build.

Sixth, do not wait for the hype to settle. Even if AI adoption plateaus tomorrow, the architecture that serves AI is also the correct architecture for human consumption. Flat tables with explicit definitions are better for dashboards, analysts, new hires, regulatory compliance, and audit trails.

You are not optimizing for a trend. You are optimizing for precision. AI is the forcing function that makes precision non-negotiable. The architecture was always correct. The economics now demand it.

Organizations that understand this will have data platforms that serve any consumer —- human or machine —- with consistent, governed, trustworthy answers. Their AI agents will answer business questions reliably because the semantic infrastructure exists to ground them. Their analysts will stop squinting at conflicting dashboards because there is one governed definition, not forty. Their new hires will understand the data estate in their first week because the vocabulary is explicit and the definitions are machine-readable, which also means they are human-readable. Everything gets better.

Organizations that do not will continue building dashboards that humans squint at, deploying AI projects that join the 60 percent abandoned due to bad data. They will spend millions on AI initiatives and wonder why the results are disappointing. They will blame the models. The models were never the problem.

Intelligent machines are interning at your organization — if you want to keep them on as productive long-term employees, get your training material together.

== Language is the Material

=== The Column Name Problem

Fourteen million dollars is a lot of money to lose in a dictionary. The quarterly P&L reconciliation broke on a Tuesday —- not because the numbers were wrong; the numbers were perfect, every decimal in its place. It broke because the finance team’s chart of accounts classified a particular set of flows as "trading revenue," while the trading system that originated those same flows classified them as "market-making fees." Both were correct. Both had documentation. Both had been in production for years. And both produced a P&L that differed by fourteen million dollars depending on which system you asked.

Three hours of meeting followed. A finance director and a trading desk lead sat across from each other, each with a spreadsheet, each convinced the other’s numbers were wrong. They were not wrong. They were speaking different languages. "Revenue" in the finance register included fee rebates netted against gross. "Revenue" in the trading register excluded them. The word was identical. The concept was not. Fourteen million dollars lived in the gap between two definitions of the same term.

Every automated check passed. Every pipeline ran green. The conflict was invisible to every tool in the stack because every tool operates on syntax, and syntactically the two uses of "revenue" were identical. Semantic. Linguistic. And until that Tuesday, completely undetected.

Most organizations cannot see the distinction between "missing data" and "missing vocabulary." They blame the pipeline. They almost never blame the names. The blindness is expensive: the fix for missing data is re-ingestion, which takes weeks; the fix for missing vocabulary is a definition update, which takes minutes.

AI is the forcing function, but the problem is architectural. Data engineering is not about pipelines, transformations, or SQL. Those are implementation details. The material of the craft —- the thing data engineers are actually shaping, every day, whether they realize it or not —- is language.

=== Names Construct the Objects They Refer To

Most data engineers think of naming as a style decision. Pick a convention —- snake_case, camelCase, with prefixes, without —- enforce it, move on. This is the standard position, and it is wrong.

When you name a column `revenue`, you are not labeling a pre-existing thing. You are making a commitment: this number, calculated this way, from these sources, with these exclusions, means THIS. Before the name, there was raw data —- transactions, line items, adjustments, reversals. After the name, there is a governed concept that downstream consumers can rely on. Naming is construction.

If a definition is a contract —- "when you use this term, you are bound to this meaning" —- then breaking the contract breaks every downstream consumer. When AI is the downstream consumer, breaking the contract does not produce a confused analyst who calls you on Slack. It produces a confident, silent, wrong answer that gets presented to an executive or, worse, acted upon by an automated system.

At a major investment bank, the word "position" meant different things to the risk desk, the trading desk, and the back office. Three definitions, all internally consistent, all mutually incompatible. Humans navigated this by knowing which desk they were talking to. The data warehouse did not know which desk it was talking to. And so the reporting layer contained three different versions of "position," each correct within its own context, each dangerously wrong when used in someone else’s.

Industry treats this as a data quality problem. It is a language problem —- specifically, polysemy: a single term carrying multiple meanings depending on context. Linguists have studied polysemy for a century. Data engineers rediscover it every time they build a warehouse.

Consider what happened with a chart of accounts at a crypto exchange. Three hundred and thirty-three GL accounts, which sounds reasonable until you realize they encode roughly forty business concepts crossed with six dimensions: asset class, related party status, client type, product, counterparty, and risk level. Instead of separating the concept from its dimensional context, the naming scheme bakes context into the identifier. Account number 41200-003-01 is not a concept —- it is a concept wearing a disguise made of dimensions. A human accountant can decode this because they have twenty years of tacit knowledge. An AI agent asked to "show me all revenue accounts" will miss half of them because the revenue concept is not explicit in the name but implicit in a dimensional encoding that nobody documented.

Naming-as-governance in miniature: three hundred and thirty-three entries when forty archetypes would suffice with proper vocabulary architecture. The naming scheme encodes too many dimensions into a single identifier, making it opaque to both humans and machines.

Kimball prefixes —- `fact_`, `dim_`, `fct_` —- are the same kind of mistake. They were governance decisions, correct at the time they were made. In row-based databases where joins were expensive, encoding the structural role of a table into its name was useful operational metadata. It told the query optimizer and the developer what to expect. But we do not live in that world anymore. In columnar warehouses (BigQuery, Snowflake, Databricks — the specific engine is immaterial), denormalized wide tables outperform star schemas. A semantic layer determines whether a field is a dimension or a measure at the field level, not the table level. The prefix is redundant —- a governance decision that outlived the architecture it was governing.

Naming conventions are governance decisions, and governance decisions should be revisited when the architecture changes. Calling something `fact_orders` in a columnar database with a semantic layer is like requiring a card catalog in a library with a search engine. The card catalog was brilliant for its constraint. The constraint expired.

Namespaces are the deeper point. A namespace is not a naming convention. It is the unit of governance. IAM policies, quality rules, and ownership are defined at the namespace level. A table named `trading.orders` inherits different governance than one named `staging_trading.raw_orders`. The name determines who can read the data, who can write to it, what quality checks apply, and who is accountable when something breaks. When you name a table, you are choosing a governance regime, not a label.

Naming decisions are among the most consequential decisions a data architect makes. Yet in most organizations, naming is delegated to individual developers with at most a style guide. Delegating contract law to the intern: the contracts still get written, they just do not protect anyone.

Sometimes the names are not just unclear —- they are actively misleading. A column called `volume` that contains notional value. A `balance` that means delta since last update, not current state. A `date` that refers to trade date in one system and settlement date in another. In each case, the name was plausible enough that nobody questioned it. The AI certainly did not question it —- it used the column at face value and produced results that were internally consistent but factually wrong. Missing names produce errors you notice. Misleading names produce errors you trust.

=== The Metadata Graveyard

Every large organization has one. Ten thousand terms in a governance tool that nobody reads. A business glossary delivered to applause and abandoned within a year. The pattern is so consistent it qualifies as a law of organizational physics.

A senior leader decides the organization needs a business glossary. Consultants arrive. Five information architects, maybe more, each pulling down $150,000 to $250,000 a year. Workshops happen. Over six to twelve months, a comprehensive glossary is produced —- hundreds or thousands of terms, each carefully defined, each vetted. Published in a standalone governance tool. Launch event. Applause. And then, slowly, silently, it dies.

It dies because modeling is a maintenance problem disguised as a design problem. A vocabulary is a living system, not a deliverable. Data platforms change daily. New tables appear. Columns are renamed. Business concepts evolve. Models are deprecated and replaced. A vocabulary that is not updated with every change is a vocabulary that drifts from reality. And a vocabulary that drifts from reality is worse than no vocabulary at all, because it creates the illusion of governance where none exists.

Gartner estimates that 80 percent of data and analytics governance initiatives will fail by 2027 due to a lack of connection to business outcomes. As many as 90 percent fail at the first attempt. Poor data quality costs organizations an average of $12.9 million per year. Users lose 30 to 40 percent of their time simply trying to find and understand information that already exists in their organization. The metadata graveyard is the default outcome, not a niche problem.

A senior data leader described traditional data catalogs as "expensive shelfware." Another: "Let’s visit a website just to browse the metadata, said no one ever." Nobody browses metadata recreationally. If the definitions are not in the path of the work —- if you have to leave your IDE, open a browser, navigate to a catalog, and search for a term —- you will not do it. And if you will not do it, the definitions might as well not exist.

FIBO —- the Financial Industry Business Ontology —- represents the most ambitious attempt to solve the vocabulary problem in financial services, and it deserves fair treatment before the critique. It has over three thousand normative entities. It was built by contributors from Bloomberg, Citigroup, Deutsche Bank, Goldman Sachs, Wells Fargo, and State Street. It is maintained by the EDM Council. It is formally exquisite, built on OWL and RDF, with proper axioms and reasoning capabilities. It represents years of work by people who understood the problem deeply.

Almost nobody uses it. Broader industry adoption remains limited. Ontologists with expertise in both OWL and finance are rare enough to be practically nonexistent. Scaling FIBO adoption beyond small Centers of Excellence has proven consistently difficult. When researchers tried to run standard OWL reasoners against the full FIBO ontology, one was terminated after four days of processing. Another after two. Formally beautiful, operationally inert.

OWL and RDF more broadly are not faring much better. A W3C GitHub issue from a few years ago asked bluntly: "Which real use cases are there for RDF/OWL ontologies?" The author noted that after years of working with the technology, they had become "more and more unsure why someone should bother with them." Even the European Union’s Core Public Event Vocabulary —- an official standard, built by specialists —- contains dozens of OWL 2 DL violations. If the standards bodies themselves cannot produce clean OWL, expecting commercial data teams to do so is unrealistic. The emerging consensus among practitioners is telling: not one universal ontology language, but "federation, not uniformity" —- lightweight, domain-specific vocabularies connected through explicit mappings rather than through formal OWL reasoning.

Formalism is sound. Failure is in the distance between formalism and operational pipeline. An ontology that does not execute in the data pipeline does not govern the data. It describes the data from the outside, and descriptions from the outside drift. Any vocabulary system that exists separately from the systems it governs suffers this fate. The "boil the ocean" anti-pattern —- one comprehensive ontology covering all business domains before shipping anything —- is the most reliable way to spend two years and deliver nothing.

Standalone governance tools suffer the same critique with less severity. Collibra is governance-first: policy modeling, enterprise vocabularies, lifecycle management. Implementation timelines run three to nine months. User satisfaction among those who actually use the tool is high —- around 87 percent. But satisfaction among users is a different metric than adoption across the organization. The governance team is twelve people. The organization has three thousand. The other 2,988 have never logged in.

Both vendors are pivoting toward AI —- Collibra acquiring companies focused on unstructured data governance for LLMs, Alation acquiring firms specializing in AI agents over structured data. The irony is delicious: governance tools racing to embed AI at the same moment AI is making their original architecture obsolete.

Neither the heavyweight ontology nor the standalone catalog is the villain. The villain is any architecture that puts governance outside the execution pipeline. Collibra is right that governance matters; wrong that it should live in a separate tool. FIBO is right that formal vocabulary matters; wrong that formal vocabulary requires OWL.

A deeper problem lurks beneath the catalog debate. The controlled-vocabulary tradition —- formalized in ISO 11179 and instantiated in every modern data catalog product —- was built for cross-reference, schema standardization, and demonstrable compliance. Real goals, well served. But the artefact is wrong, not just the maintenance model.

A short, structured definition tells you the datatype of a column and what the column is called. It does not tell you why the column exists, when its values diverge from the obvious interpretation, what edge cases bite practitioners every quarter, what the desk’s slang for it is, how it relates to upstream system quirks, or what a new analyst should know on their second day. All of this is information that exists in the heads of senior practitioners. It is precisely the information the controlled-vocabulary tradition has, throughout its history, failed to capture.

Tolerable when documentation consumers were humans who could absorb operational knowledge by sitting next to senior colleagues for six months. You learned the quirks of the P&L table by asking the person who built it. You learned that "the book" meant something specific in this office by overhearing it in meetings for three months. You learned which columns were vestigial by watching what the senior analysts actually used. None of this knowledge appeared in any catalog. It did not need to, because the humans who needed it had a channel —- proximity, conversation, apprenticeship —- through which to acquire it.

No such channel exists for an LLM agent. It has whatever you put in its context and nothing more. If the documentation says "revenue: total trading revenue in USD" and nothing else, that is the entirety of what the agent knows about revenue. It does not know that revenue excludes maker rebates in Q1 but includes them from Q2 onward due to a policy change. It does not know that the upstream system occasionally double-counts fee reversals during month-end settlement windows. It does not know that the desk calls the field "top-line" and never uses the word "revenue" in conversation. Formally correct. Operationally useless. The agent will produce answers consistent with the definition and inconsistent with reality.

Metadata graveyards were always failing to close this gap, even for human consumers. AI merely makes the failure impossible to ignore.

=== Documentation as Onboarding

ISO 11179 tells you a column’s type. A senior colleague tells you why the column lies on the last day of the month. Only one of these prevents a production incident.

When a new analyst joins a trading desk, nobody hands them a metadata catalog. They get prose, and they get conversation. Someone tells them the war stories: the time the upstream system reclassified counterparties without warning and broke the morning P&L for a week. Someone explains the conventions: how P&L is computed here, which of the three canonical definitions the desk uses, when it switches between them. Someone walks them through the slang: what "the book" means in this office, which fields are actually used and which are vestigial. The onboarding is narrative, contextual, and specific to how this particular team uses these particular data assets.

A capable LLM brings substantial general knowledge to any business domain. Accounting principles, market mechanics, regulatory shape, the standard patterns of analytical work in finance —- on day one, that general knowledge frequently exceeds what a junior human analyst arrives with. A fresh graduate joining a treasury desk does not typically know the mechanics of tri-party repo or the regulatory treatment of rehypothecated collateral. The LLM does. What it lacks is what the human will spend the next month acquiring: organizational nuance, desk-specific convention, tribal slang, the operational craft of how this particular team uses these particular data assets.

After about a month, the human analyst has absorbed enough context to function. They know which of the three revenue definitions the desk actually uses. They know that "the cross" means the cross-currency basis swap book, not the FX cross rate. They know that the 4pm snapshot is the one that matters and the 3:30pm snapshot is vestigial but still produced because a downstream system depends on it. Without equivalent context delivered explicitly, the agent plateaus at general competence —- correct in the abstract, wrong in the particular. The particular is where the work happens.

Documentation that closes this gap looks like onboarding material, not ISO 11179.

What works is the domain essay: six hundred to fifteen hundred words of considered prose covering provenance, conventions, edge cases, slang, history, and craft for a single dataset or a tightly coupled cluster of them. Written by the people who know the data —- not generated from the schema, which by construction cannot tell you what the schema fails to capture. The essay is anchored on structured metadata that does not disappear: datatypes, primary keys, refresh cadence, lineage references, ownership records. These anchors are the index into the documentation, not the documentation itself.

Structured metadata —- the ISO 11179 anchors of datatype, key, classification —- remains essential. Machine-readable, automatable, suitable for cross-reference and integration. But the shift is in where the semantic weight lives. In an agentic architecture, the essay is the primary carrier of meaning. Structured metadata becomes the index into the essay, not the documentation itself.

A domain essay is not chatty documentation. It is the operational note a senior practitioner writes for a new colleague: dense, deliberate, evidence-based, free of padding. Prose rather than controlled-vocabulary form —- a different format for a different consumer, not a lower standard.

Essay-based documentation works because it follows the discipline of code. Versioned. Dated revisions. Named authors and reviewers. Stored alongside the data assets it describes. And a decay model: an essay reviewed within the last twelve months is treated differently from one untouched for three years. Stale documentation is, for an agent, strictly worse than absent documentation —- the agent reads stale prose with the same confidence as fresh prose and produces confidently wrong answers. A documentation system that does not manage decay produces worse outcomes the longer it operates.
