Documentation that lets an agent reason over data is documentation that onboards a new human analyst fastest. The agent, the new analyst, and the auditor converge on the same artefact. Build documentation for agents and it gets better for humans. Build it for cataloging and it has never been good enough for either.

=== The Three-Tier Vocabulary

Three tiers, connected by a single principle: upstream always wins.


[#fig-three-tier-vocab]
.The three-tier vocabulary: glossary governs schema governs semantic layer. Upstream always wins.
image::figures/three-tier-vocab.png[three tier vocab,width=600,align="center"]

Tier 1 is the glossary. This is the canonical source of business meaning. It contains the organization’s controlled vocabulary: defined terms, with relationships between them —- hierarchies, synonyms, associations. The glossary is business-owned, not technology-owned. It does not contain SQL. It does not reference specific tables or columns. It speaks in business language about business concepts. "Trading volume" is defined here. "Revenue" is defined here. "Position" is defined here, with enough precision that two reasonable people reading the definition would agree on what it means.

Terms are not strings. They are nodes in a semantic graph. "Revenue" does not exist in isolation. It exists in relation to "volume," "position," "trade," "fee," "rebate." Meaning is partly constituted by relationships between terms. A glossary that treats terms as isolated definitions —- a flat list with descriptions —- is missing the point. A vocabulary that treats terms as a network, with explicit relationships, captures how meaning actually works in a business domain. An AI agent that knows "revenue" is a type of "financial metric" that is calculated from "trades" and excludes "rebates" can reason about revenue in ways that an agent with just a string definition cannot.

Tools constrain what ontologies can express. A YAML file in a code repository yields flat, static, developer-facing text. A governed system with relational semantics yields hierarchies, synonyms, associations, and machine-queryable relationships. Not OWL —- but something richer than a flat file.

Tier 2 is the schema definition file — the model metadata that lives alongside the transformation code (dbt’s `schema.yml` in the current implementation, though the principle applies to any SQL-based modelling framework). Here is the claim that will generate the most pushback: the schema definition IS the logical data model — not a reflection of it, not a downstream copy, but the thing itself.

In traditional data architecture, the logical data model is a separate artifact —- an ER diagram, a data dictionary, a design document —- created during design and then implemented in code. Two artifacts, one truth, inevitable divergence. Every data architect has lived this: a beautiful ER diagram on Confluence that was accurate eighteen months ago. The actual tables look nothing like it. Nobody updates the diagram because nobody looks at it.

When model documentation and model implementation are the same file, they cannot diverge. You cannot change the model without changing the documentation, because they are the same artifact. The schema is not a description of the data from outside —- it constitutes the data’s meaning. A schema change is not a "metadata update." It is a material transformation of what the data can mean.

"But we need design review before implementation." Yes —- and in a CI/CD pipeline with code review, the schema definition IS reviewed before deployment. There is no useful separation between "design" and "implementation" when the design artifact is executable. A separate LDM document is not "design." It is a copy. Copies drift. This one will too.

Tier 2 inherits from Tier 1. If the glossary says "trading volume" means the total number of units traded in a given period, then the schema description of the `trading_volume` column says the same thing — not a paraphrase, not a reinterpretation, but the same definition. Upstream always wins. If a Tier 2 description contradicts a Tier 1 definition, the Tier 1 definition is correct and the Tier 2 description must be updated. This is not a suggestion. It is a governance rule, and it must be enforced —- ideally by automation, practically by code review, minimally by convention.

Tier 3 is the semantic layer —- the consumption-layer configuration that maps business concepts to data (LookML, dbt Semantic Layer, Cube, or equivalent). This is where definitions become queryable. The semantic layer does not create new definitions. It inherits them from Tier 2, which inherited them from Tier 1. What the semantic layer adds is navigation: how concepts relate to each other for the purpose of answering questions, what the default aggregations are, what filters are commonly applied, what hierarchies structure the data for exploration.

When an AI agent needs to answer a question about revenue, it consults the semantic layer, which tells it which table contains revenue data, how revenue is calculated, what dimensions are available, what caveats apply. Without a semantic layer, the agent guesses —- picking the wrong column, inventing a calculation, joining tables incorrectly. With one, it grounds its reasoning in governed definitions. The difference between guessing and grounding is whether you built the vocabulary.

When AI is the consumer, naming decisions have cost implications that make CFOs pay attention. A star schema requires the AI to reason about joins —- which tables to connect, through which keys, in what order. Every join is additional tokens consumed, additional reasoning steps, additional opportunities for error. A flat, denormalized Gold table with a semantic layer requires no join reasoning. The AI reads the semantic layer, identifies the right table, and queries it directly. In practice, this reduces token consumption by a factor of three to five. Your naming conventions and architectural choices are cost decisions, not just semantic ones. Bad names are expensive.

A quality standard applies across all three tiers. ISO 11179 —- the international standard for metadata registries —- provides criteria that every definition should meet: precise, unambiguous, non-circular, stated in natural language, domain-scoped. The standard has been actively maintained since the 1990s and was updated most recently in 2023 and 2024. It is the foundational reference for metadata in government, healthcare, and regulated industries. Australia, Canada, Germany, the US, and the UK all maintain ISO 11179-aligned registries. A Texas state agency published a standard grounded in 11179 that begins with a statement so obvious it should not need saying: "To be data-driven, an organization must be metadata-driven."

ISO 11179’s problem is not the standard —- the standard is excellent. Organizations treat it as a compliance exercise rather than a quality tool. The three-tier vocabulary uses 11179 criteria the way a newsroom uses a style guide: not as bureaucracy, but as craft discipline. Every definition should pass 11179 scrutiny. But the definitions live in the pipeline, not in a separate registry. The standard governs the quality. The architecture governs the location.

Domain essays carry the semantic weight. Structured metadata becomes the index into essays, not the documentation itself. Tier 1 names the concepts, Tier 2 binds them to columns, Tier 3 exposes them to consumers. But the explanation of why a column exists, the documentation of edge cases that bite every quarter, the operational craft that separates a competent analyst from a textbook one —- that lives in the essay alongside and above the tiers.

"Upstream always wins" extends to documentation. The glossary defines the term. The schema definition binds it. The semantic layer exposes it. But the essay —- written by domain experts, versioned like code, decaying visibly when stale —- is what the agent reads when it needs to reason, not retrieve. Retrieval asks "what is this column called and what is its datatype?" Reasoning asks "why does this column behave unexpectedly during month-end, and what should a competent analyst do about it?" Only the essay answers the second question. The tiers are the skeleton. The essay is the knowledge.

This architecture works because it solves the maintenance problem that kills every other approach. The glossary changes slowly —- business concepts evolve over quarters and years, not days. The schema definition changes at the pace of development —- when models are added, modified, or deprecated. The semantic layer changes at the pace of consumption —- when new questions arise or new navigational paths are needed. Each tier has its own cadence, its own owners, and its own change management process. But they are connected by a single flow: upstream always wins. This means governance becomes an architectural property, not a separate activity. The system governs itself through its own structure.

Compare this to the standalone governance tool. Collibra, or any similar platform, sits outside the execution pipeline. Definitions in Collibra do not automatically propagate to the schema definition or the semantic layer. When a developer changes a column name in the transformation tool, they do not automatically update Collibra. Two systems, two truths, inevitable drift. The three-tier vocabulary eliminates this drift not through discipline but through architecture. The tiers are connected. Changes propagate. Upstream always wins. It is governance by construction, not governance by committee.

Governance outside the execution pipeline is aspiration, not governance. It exists as documentation. It exists as a quarterly meeting where people review proposed changes to the data dictionary and approve or reject them. By the time a change is approved through a committee process, the data has already changed three more times. The committee is governing a system that no longer exists. Governance by committee is governance by nostalgia.

*Pattern: Three-Tier Vocabulary*

_Context:_ An organization needs governed definitions that flow consistently from business language to data infrastructure to consumption interfaces.

_Problem:_ Without a tiered architecture, definitions are maintained independently at each level — the glossary says one thing, the schema describes another, and the semantic layer presents a third. Contradictions accumulate silently.

_Solution:_ Three tiers, with upstream always winning. Tier 1 (Business Glossary): canonical definitions owned by business stakeholders, stored in a machine-readable format. Tier 2 (Schema Definition): transformation tool schema files inherit Tier 1 definitions verbatim — the schema description IS the glossary definition, not a paraphrase. Tier 3 (Semantic Model): the consumption-layer definitions inherit from Tier 2. No tier may introduce its own definition. No downstream system may override an upstream meaning. Changes propagate top-down: change the glossary, the schema description updates, the semantic model updates.

_Example:_ Glossary defines "trading_volume" as "total quantity of assets exchanged in a given period, excluding test accounts and internal transfers." The schema definition file for `fct_executions` inherits this definition verbatim on the `trading_volume` measure. The semantic model’s `trading_volume` measure inherits from the schema. An analyst querying through any interface sees the same definition. A change to the definition happens once, in Tier 1, and propagates automatically.

_Consequences:_ Upstream-always-wins is rigid. Domains cannot experiment with local terminology without a glossary change. Fast-moving teams feel constrained.

=== Why This Is Linguistics

"Data engineering is applied linguistics" is not a metaphor. The problems data engineers encounter every day are problems that linguists named and studied decades ago.

Synonymy is the most visible: multiple terms for the same concept. Every controlled vocabulary is an attempt to eliminate synonyms within a specific domain. When a data platform has three names for the same metric, it has a synonymy problem. The solution is not "pick one and rename the others." The solution is a controlled vocabulary that maps all three names to a single canonical term, preserving the synonyms as aliases for discoverability while enforcing the canonical term for governance.

"Position" at the investment bank is polysemy: one term with multiple meanings depending on context. Polysemy is a feature of natural language that makes communication efficient in context and disastrous out of context. A data platform is, by definition, out of context —- it aggregates data from multiple contexts into a single layer. If your vocabulary does not disambiguate polysemous terms, your platform inherits every ambiguity from every source system.

GL taxonomy inflation is a failure of dimensional separation: encoding multiple independent dimensions into a single identifier. Linguists call this agglutination —- think of German compound nouns that pack multiple concepts into a single word. In a chart of accounts, agglutinating asset class, client type, and risk level into a single account number creates the same problem: the concepts are present but not individually addressable. You cannot ask "show me all revenue accounts" because "revenue" is not a separate axis in the naming scheme; it is fused into an opaque compound.

AI returning zeros for collateral values because the table was not labeled properly —- a referential gap. The data existed. The concept existed. The mapping between them was missing. Organizations blame the data when the problem is the name.

Data engineers face linguistic problems and need linguistic tools: controlled vocabularies, taxonomies, thesauri, semantic networks. Twenty years of investment in pipeline tools —- orchestration, transformation, testing —- and comparatively nothing in vocabulary tools. The imbalance reflects a conceptual misunderstanding about what the job actually is.

Consider the job posting for a "Senior Data Engineer" at any major company. SQL, Python, Spark, Airflow, dbt, Terraform, Kubernetes. Maybe a cloud certification. Nowhere: "ability to construct and maintain a controlled vocabulary." Nowhere: "experience with semantic modeling." Nowhere: "skill in extracting precise definitions from domain experts." Yet those are the skills that determine whether the data platform produces clarity or confusion. The industry hires for pipeline competence and wonders why the vocabulary is a mess.

Industry is starting to notice. Common vocabulary as a foundational principle of modern data architecture is accelerating as a recognized prerequisite, but the recognition is still mostly rhetorical. Investment —- in tools, in people, in organizational attention —- has not caught up.

Without formal vocabulary infrastructure, business meaning scatters across schema descriptions, semantic layer definitions, tribal knowledge, and Slack threads. Everybody talking, nobody speaking the same language.

At one organization, the revenue mapping rules lived in a CSV file that nobody owned. Seven trading symbols were missing from the CSV, which meant trades in those assets were generating no journal entries. Nobody had noticed because the engineers who wrote the code did not understand the domain, and the domain experts had left. Code owned execution. Nobody owned semantics. Revenue was not a governed concept. It was a prayer.

Vocabulary architecture prevents this. When "revenue" has a definition that is canonical, machine-readable, and embedded in the execution pipeline, code and meaning cannot separate. Read the model, read the definition. Change the model, change the definition. Same artifact.

When vocabulary is not formalized, every question routes through a person. "What does this column mean?" Ask Sam. "How is this calculated?" Ask Keith. "Why does this number not match the other number?" Ask whoever built the original dashboard, if they still work here. Knowledge lives in people’s heads, and every query becomes a dependency on a specific individual.

One team lead: "My team literally don’t know the domain. Sam and Keith are the only guys that know. And that’s a problem." It is a problem —- and a predictable consequence of an architecture that does not capture vocabulary. When vocabulary exists only in people’s heads, personnel changes are data losses.

Most organizations hit a phase transition —- scaling from one office to multiple, or private to public —- where tacit knowledge stops working. When everyone sat in the same room, tacit knowledge worked —- proximity created shared context. When teams go global, tacit knowledge breaks. The meaning has to be written down, and if it is written down in a PDF on SharePoint, it will be lost within six months. It has to be written down in the pipeline.

Consider that the U.S. Department of Defense —- twenty-nine languages, twenty-four countries —- published a controlled vocabulary framework that makes this point with military directness: "When terms are not immediately and unerringly understood by every enterprise participant, there is a significant risk that process outcomes and analytic results will be inconsistent." Replace "enterprise participant" with "AI agent" and you have the thesis of this essay. If the DoD understands that vocabulary is infrastructure, data platforms have no excuse.

Organizations that navigate this transition successfully recognize what they are really building. Not a data platform —- a language. A shared, governed, machine-readable language for talking about their business. The platform is infrastructure. The language is the product.

=== Domain Registers and Code-Switching

When a trader and an accountant both say "position," they are having two different conversations with the same word. The data warehouse, unable to tell which conversation it is in, joins both and calls the result a report.


[#fig-polysemy-position]
.Polysemy: “position” means three different things to three domains. Without a semantic layer the warehouse joins them into nonsense.
image::figures/polysemy-position.png[polysemy position,width=600,align="center"]

Sociolinguists use the term "register" to describe a systematic variation in language that correlates with context of use: vocabulary, grammar, and assumptions shift depending on who is speaking, to whom, and about what. A doctor discussing a patient with a colleague uses a different register than the same doctor explaining the same condition to the patient. Same information, different encoding. Competence lies in knowing which register to deploy and when.

Every business domain operates in its own register. Trading speaks in a register of immediacy and exposure: positions are open or closed, risk is directional, time is measured in milliseconds or at most trading sessions. Finance speaks in a register of periodicity and accountability: the same underlying activity becomes a journal entry, a line in a ledger, a component of a P&L that must reconcile to the cent at month-end. Treasury speaks in a register of liquidity and obligation: what matters is not what was traded but what is owed, what is available, what can be pledged. Custody speaks in a register of safekeeping and proof: the asset is held or it is not, and the chain of custody must be unbroken and auditable.

These are not merely different vocabularies layered over a shared reality. They are different ways of constructing that reality. When a trader says "position," the word denotes a live directional exposure —- something with a mark-to-market value right now, changing with every tick. When a finance professional says "position," the word denotes a net accounting exposure —- aggregated across trades, netted by instrument and counterparty, valued at a point in time for reporting. The trader’s position is kinetic. The accountant’s position is archival. Same word. Meaning constituted by register.

Linguistics calls this code-switching: the systematic shift between registers that competent speakers navigate unconsciously but that machines need explicit mappings to handle. A senior analyst who has worked across trading and finance for a decade can code-switch mid-sentence without effort —- "the position generated a journal entry that affected the P&L position" —- and every human in the room understands that the first "position" is trading-register and the second is finance-register. The shift is unmarked; context disambiguates. A machine cannot perform this disambiguation unless the vocabulary infrastructure explicitly models domain scoping. Without it, the machine treats both occurrences as the same concept, because they are the same string. From that false identity: wrong joins, wrong aggregations, wrong numbers presented with full confidence.

Cross-domain polysemy is not an exception in the three-tier vocabulary —- it is a structural feature. Tier 1, the glossary, defines domain-scoped entries. `finance.position` and `trading.position` are different concepts that share a lexeme. No single definition broad enough to cover both. Definitional broadening —- "position: a quantity of an asset held or owed by an entity" —- is the hallmark of a vocabulary that has given up on precision. It covers everything and means nothing. Instead, the glossary maintains separate entries, each precise within its domain, with an explicit relationship between them: these terms share a lexeme but differ in domain scope, calculation method, temporal granularity, and governance owner.

Tier 2 implements each domain-scoped concept as a model in its respective namespace. The trading domain has `trading.positions` with fields that reflect the trading register: instrument, direction, entry price, current mark, unrealized P&L. The finance domain has `finance.positions` with fields that reflect the finance register: instrument, net quantity, book value, fair value, accounting standard applied. The schema definitions make explicit what human speakers leave implicit. They are, in effect, the grammar of each register written out in full.

Tier 3, the semantic layer, provides the navigation that resolves which "position" the user means based on conversational context. When a user asks "what is our BTC position?" the answer depends entirely on register. If the question follows a series of queries about trading volume and order flow, the semantic layer should resolve to the trading register. If the question follows queries about monthly P&L and revenue recognition, it should resolve to the finance register. The semantic layer uses the accumulated context of the conversation —- domain of prior queries, metrics already referenced, dimensions already filtered —- to determine which register is active. Machine code-switching: the operational expression of the linguistic theory.

Cross-register problems are harder than within-register problems. Within the trading domain, vocabulary is relatively stable: traders agree on what "position" means because they share a register. Within finance, the same is true. The conflicts erupt at the boundaries, when a query begins in one register and must compose with another. A question like "what was the P&L impact of our largest trading positions last quarter?" requires the system to identify positions in the trading register (largest by notional exposure), then compose with the finance register (P&L impact as calculated by accounting rules), resolving the temporal mismatch (trading positions are real-time, P&L is periodic) and the aggregation mismatch (trading positions are per-instrument, P&L may be per-book or per-entity). This is register composition, and it is where most data platforms fail silently.

Silent failure, because each domain’s numbers are individually correct. The error emerges only in the composition —- in the join, in the mapping, in the assumption that "position" in one system corresponds to "position" in another. A human analyst with cross-domain experience catches this because they can code-switch, because they know that the trading system’s position and the finance system’s position are related but not identical. An analyst without that experience, or an AI agent without explicit register mappings, will join on the shared lexeme and produce a number that looks right and is not.

Domain registers are an architectural requirement, not a theoretical nicety. Each domain is a register. Each register has its own controlled vocabulary. Cross-register mappings are explicit, governed artifacts —- not implicit assumptions buried in join logic. When a Gold table combines data from trading and finance, the schema definition must document which register each field belongs to and how the cross-register composition was resolved. Documentation here is not commentary —- it is the semantic contract that makes the composition trustworthy.

At a crypto exchange, register boundaries are particularly sharp. A single trade generates events that propagate through four registers almost simultaneously. In the trading register, it is an execution: instrument, price, quantity, timestamp, counterparty. In the custody register, it is a settlement obligation: assets must move from one wallet to another, with cryptographic proof. In the finance register, it is a revenue event: the fee is recognized, the journal entry is created, the P&L impact is calculated. In the treasury register, it is a liquidity event: the exchange’s available reserves have changed, and the treasury position must be updated. One trade, four registers, four vocabularies, four sets of assumptions about what matters and what does not. A data platform serving all four domains must be explicitly multilingual —- four registers, four sets of domain-scoped definitions, and a composition layer that maps between them.

A single flat vocabulary with one definition of each term is the approach most organizations attempt and most regret. It produces definitions so broad they are useless, or definitions so narrow they apply to only one domain while misleading the others. Register-based vocabulary is harder to build —- more definitions, more explicit mappings, more governance. But it produces a vocabulary that reflects how the business actually operates: a federation of domains, each with its own register, connected through explicit and governed translations.

No natural language has a single register. English does not speak the same way in a courtroom, a laboratory, a trading floor, and a kitchen. A competent data platform must navigate the same shifts —- not through a large language model that approximates register-awareness from training data, but through explicit vocabulary infrastructure that models registers as first-class architectural concepts.

*Pattern: Domain Register*

_Context:_ Multiple business domains use overlapping vocabulary — the same word means different things in different domains.

_Problem:_ Cross-domain queries silently conflate domain-specific meanings. "Position" in Trading (current holdings) is not "position" in Treasury (cash and liquidity allocation). An agent or analyst using the wrong definition produces wrong answers without warning.

_Solution:_ Each domain operates in a linguistic register — a self-consistent vocabulary with its own conventions, slang, and default assumptions. The register is documented explicitly: which terms are domain-specific, what they mean in this domain, and how they differ from the same term in other domains. When a query crosses domains, register switching is explicit — the system loads the target domain’s register and resolves any polysemous terms through the bridge morphism.

_Example:_ Trading register: "position" = current holdings by instrument. Treasury register: "position" = cash and liquidity allocation by currency. When an agent encounters "position" in a cross-domain query, the register switching protocol identifies which domain’s definition applies. If both are needed, the bridge morphism documents how Trading.position and Treasury.position relate (and where they do not).

_Consequences:_ Maintaining explicit register documentation for every domain is ongoing work. Registers drift if not reviewed.

*Pattern: Polysemy Resolution*

_Context:_ A term appears in multiple domain registers with different meanings.

_Problem:_ Without explicit resolution, the system picks one meaning silently — typically whichever table appears first in the schema or whichever domain the agent was initialized in. The consumer does not know which meaning was used.

_Solution:_ Polysemous terms are flagged in the vocabulary with explicit cross-domain mappings. Each domain’s definition stands within its register. The bridge morphism between domains documents how the definitions relate: whether they are the same concept viewed differently (Trading.Instrument ≈ Finance.FinancialProduct), overlapping but non-identical (Trading.volume ≠ Finance.revenue_volume), or unrelated homonyms (Trading.book ≠ Accounting.book). When an agent or semantic layer encounters a polysemous term in a cross-domain context, the resolution is governed and declared, not inferred.

_Example:_ "Revenue" in Finance = net recognized revenue per GAAP. "Revenue" in Trading = gross execution value. An agent asked "What is our revenue?" in a cross-domain context loads both definitions, identifies the polysemy, and either asks the consumer which definition they mean or defaults to the requesting domain’s register with an explicit declaration: "Using Finance definition of revenue (net recognized, GAAP). Trading uses a different definition (gross execution value)."

_Consequences:_ Cross-domain queries become slower (resolution overhead) and more complex to debug when polysemy maps conflict.

=== Why Data Engineering Is Applied Linguistics

Type systems and semantic layers are structurally isomorphic, and making this explicit reframes what data architecture actually is. A type system —- the kind studied in programming language theory and mathematical logic —- tells you what operations are valid on what objects. You cannot add a string to a number, not because the computer cannot do it, but because the type system says it is meaningless. A semantic layer does the same thing for data: it tells you what queries are valid on what tables, what aggregations make sense for what measures, what hierarchies structure what dimensions. A type system and a semantic layer are the same discipline applied at different levels of abstraction. (This is not incidental to the author’s perspective —- a background in type theory tends to make this structural parallel impossible to unsee.)

Data architecture is, at its core, the management of ontological commitments. When you define a Gold table, you are committing to a specific way of carving up the world. "These are our customers. These are our orders. These are our positions." Those categories are not given by nature. They are constructed by your modeling decisions. A different organization, looking at the same raw data, would construct different categories. A different modeler within the same organization would construct slightly different categories —- not wrong, just choices. But choices that, once made, constrain everything downstream. Every query, every dashboard, every AI agent reasons within the constraints of your ontological commitments. If the commitments are wrong, everything downstream is wrong. Not because the pipeline is broken, but because the vocabulary is broken.

People who understand that meaning is constructed, that definitions are contractual, that the material of the work is language itself —- linguists, librarians, logicians, or engineers who think like them —- should be shaping these commitments. Pipelines matter. SQL matters. Orchestration matters. But the hardest part of data architecture is not the transformation —- it is the vocabulary. It is deciding what "revenue" means, where the boundary between "trading" and "market-making" falls, whether "position" is one concept or three. These are linguistic decisions with engineering consequences, and the industry has systematically underinvested in the people and tools needed to make them well.

"This sounds great in theory, but I have two thousand tables and no glossary. Where do I even start?" Fair question. The answer has changed in the last eighteen months: the economics of curation have fundamentally shifted, and a comprehensive vocabulary that once took a dedicated team years to build is now within reach of one person working with AI. The maintenance problem that killed every previous attempt at vocabulary architecture is solvable when AI handles the maintenance.

Bottleneck has shifted from labor to quality of thinking. You still need someone who understands both the domain and the technology. You still need someone who can sit with a finance stakeholder for an hour and extract the difference between "gross revenue" and "net revenue" and "recognized revenue" and "booked revenue." You still need someone who can say: these are four different concepts, and here is why, and here is what each one means, and here is how they relate to each other. That is human work —- and the most valuable work on the data platform.

Most data platforms are filing cabinets without an index. Thousands of tables, millions of rows, terabytes of storage —- and without a vocabulary that organizes, defines, and relates the concepts those tables contain, the platform stores data the way a warehouse stores boxes: faithfully, efficiently, and without any understanding of what is inside them. Vocabulary transforms storage into knowledge.

Stack-agnostic. Examples throughout name specific tools — dbt, LookML, BigQuery — because they are drawn from practice. The three-tier vocabulary works equally well with any transformation framework, any semantic layer tool, any columnar warehouse, or any agent orchestration tool. The tools are interchangeable. The vocabulary architecture is not.

For organizations operating across borders, vocabulary architecture must account for multilingual contexts. In a global organization, business concepts may be discussed in English in one office, Mandarin in another, German in a third. The three-tier structure helps here: the glossary can hold multilingual definitions —- the same concept expressed in multiple languages —- while the schema definitions and the semantic layer operate in English as a lingua franca. The canonical term is language-independent; its expression is not. Solvable within the architecture, but a problem most vocabulary initiatives ignore until it bites.

=== Vocabulary Before Pipelines

If you are running a data platform and you think your job is about pipelines and transformations and SQL, you are describing the plumbing and ignoring the water supply. The material of the work —- the thing that determines whether the platform creates value or confusion —- is language. The words chosen for columns, tables, domains, definitions. Those words are the interface between an organization’s knowledge and every consumer of its data, human or machine.

Always true. What changed is that AI makes it undeniable. A human encountering a badly named column can ask someone, check source code, apply tribal knowledge. An AI encountering the same column either guesses or fails. No workaround. No tribal knowledge. Only what the name says and what the definition provides. If the name is misleading and the definition is missing, the AI is worse than useless —- it is confidently wrong.

Gartner estimates 60 percent of AI projects will be abandoned due to a lack of AI-ready data. Sixty-three percent of organizations either lack or are unsure they have the right data management practices for AI. Forty percent of business initiatives fail due to poor data quality. Default outcomes of building AI on ungoverned vocabulary. The technology works. The data exists. The vocabulary does not.

First: invest in vocabulary before anything else. Before the new dashboard, before the AI agent, before the transformation refactor —- define terms. Start with the twenty most important business concepts. Get stakeholders in a room and define them —- not in a PDF, not in a governance tool outside the pipeline, but in the schema definition that IS the model. Machine-readable, precise, canonical. Days, not months. Saves years.

Second: kill the metadata graveyard. A business glossary that has not been updated in six months is a liability, not a glossary. Either embed it in your execution pipeline —- connect it to your schema definitions so that changes propagate automatically —- or delete it. A stale glossary creates the illusion of governance, and the illusion is more dangerous than the absence.

Third: upstream always wins. If the glossary says "trading volume" means X, then every downstream system says "trading volume" means X — the same definition, not a paraphrase. A downstream system that wants a different concept gets a different name. "Trading volume" is taken. Rigid at the definitional level to enable flexibility at every other level.

Fourth: naming conventions are governance decisions. Review them with the same rigor applied to IAM policies —- they determine the same thing: who can do what with which data, and what the data means when they do it.

Fifth: the scarce resource on any data platform is not engineers or compute or storage. It is vocabulary —- the quality of definitions, the precision of naming, the rigor of semantic relationships. Most organizations have more people working on their transformation layer than on their vocabulary. Reverse that ratio, or at least balance it.

Technology changes enormously across years and industries. Problems do not. "What does this column mean?" "Why does this number not match that number?" "Who defined this term and when did the definition change?" Always vocabulary. Always linguistic. Regardless of stack.

Your AI agent will be exactly as intelligent as your vocabulary allows it to be. Model quality is upstream of you. Training data is upstream of you. Infrastructure is upstream of you. The one thing entirely within your control is whether you built the vocabulary —- the unglamorous, difficult, deeply human work of defining terms.

Every organization that deploys an AI agent without a governed vocabulary has built a confident new employee, handed it a desk and a database login, and forgotten to tell it what any of the words mean.

[#chap-architecture]
== The Architecture

If you are running a data platform, you are running a restaurant — one where the kitchen has no menu, the chef has never met a customer, and the health inspector is an AI that reads faster than you can lie. You have suppliers who deliver raw ingredients — some excellent, some questionable, all of it arriving on their schedule in their packaging. You have a kitchen where those ingredients are cleaned, prepped, and transformed into dishes that people actually want to eat. And you have a menu — a curated, opinionated selection of what you are willing to serve, described in language your customers understand.

Now think about most data platforms you have worked with. They have a loading dock. Trucks back up, dump ingredients, and drive away. They might have something resembling a kitchen — some transformation logic, maybe a few models in the transformation tool. But do they have a menu? Do they have a curated, described, governed set of answers to the questions their consumers actually ask?

They do not. Most data platforms are warehouses with loading docks. Ingredients come in. Ingredients pile up. Someone occasionally rummages through the pile and assembles something edible. But there is no menu, no kitchen discipline, no chef who has decided what this restaurant serves and what it does not.


[#fig-restaurant-no-menu]
.A warehouse with a loading dock and no menu: ingredients pile up, but nobody has decided what the platform serves.
image::figures/restaurant-no-menu.png[restaurant no menu,width=600,align="center"]

Medallion architecture — Bronze, Silver, Gold — is how you build the restaurant — not as three folders in your data lake, not as a data quality gradient from raw to clean. These are epistemological layers: they answer different questions about your data’s relationship to truth. Bronze asks: what did the producer say? Silver asks: what does it mean in our language? Gold asks: what do we know?

[#fig-medallion-layers]
.Medallion Architecture — Bronze, Silver, Gold, and the Semantic Layer
image::figures/medallion-layers.png[Medallion Architecture — Bronze, Silver, Gold, and the Semantic Layer,width=680,align=center]


Get these layers right, pair them with a semantic layer that acts as your menu, and you have something most organizations have never had: a knowledge architecture. Get them wrong — or worse, skip them — and you have a very expensive pile of ingredients rotting on a loading dock.

The medallion pattern has taken some hits. It was popularized as a data quality gradient — Bronze is raw, Silver is cleaned, Gold is curated — and when you frame it that way, the criticism writes itself. Practitioners complain that nobody in their company understands what "Silver" versus "Gold" actually means. Critics point out, fairly, that Bronze/Silver/Gold is just Staging/Fact/Dimension/Data Mart under new names — traditional data warehousing rebranded for the lakehouse era. One prominent voice in the community argued that the engineers who popularized medallion simply did not have the proper background in data warehousing and sold three mandatory layers to drive storage and compute revenue.

These criticisms land because they are aimed at a real target: the version of medallion architecture that most organizations implement. Three folders. Three quality tiers. No semantic infrastructure. No clear boundary definitions.

But that is not the architecture described here. Epistemological layers — each one answers a fundamentally different question about your data’s relationship to truth. Bronze is about provenance: what was said? Silver is about meaning: what does it mean in our language? Gold is about knowledge: what do we know? Whether you frame medallion as epistemological or as "raw/cleaned/curated" determines whether the architecture produces governed knowledge or just moves data through three copies.

Yes, medallion architecture stores data three times. In an era where storage costs are measured in pennies per gigabyte per month, this is not the argument the critics think it is. Three copies cost pennies. Five teams building five different interpretations of the same source data — all slightly different, none governed — costs millions. Organizations routinely spend more money reconciling contradictory Gold tables than they would have spent storing three clean copies of everything.

=== Bronze: The Faithful Witness

Bronze is not a staging area. Most people get this wrong first, and the error is structural, not cosmetic.

A staging area is a temporary holding pen. Data lands there, gets cleaned, moves on, and the staging area is wiped. Bronze is permanent. Bronze is a faithful, immutable replication of what the producer delivered, in their language, using their conventions, warts and all. When I say "faithful witness," I mean it almost in the legal sense: Bronze is the sworn testimony. You do not edit sworn testimony. You do not clean it up to make it more presentable. You record it exactly as given and move on to interpretation.


[#fig-bronze-witness]
.Bronze is sworn testimony — the producer’s record, taken down verbatim and never edited.
image::figures/bronze-witness.png[bronze witness,width=600,align="center"]

Bronze is your dirty laundry. The producer’s naming conventions might be terrible. Their data types might be inconsistent. Their schema might change without warning. All of that lands in Bronze, unmodified, because Bronze is about accountability, not your convenience.

Producers own Bronze. When a third-party system lands data in your Bronze zone, the accountability boundary is crystal clear — they own the data, you own the interpretation. This distinction matters enormously when something goes wrong. If the numbers do not reconcile, you can point to Bronze and say: this is what you gave us. The argument stops there. If you had cleaned the data on the way in, you have destroyed the evidence.

A corollary most teams resist: do not copy Bronze when you can reference it. A senior engineer proposed jobs that copied a vendor’s data into our storage. My response was direct: the whole benefit of buying this product is that they land data in a zone we can see. I do not want jobs copying this. They landed it, it is theirs. We do our own Silver.

Copying Bronze destroys the accountability boundary. It creates a second source of truth. It introduces a synchronization problem that did not need to exist. The vendor’s data, in the vendor’s format, in the vendor’s zone — that is Bronze. Touch it and you own the discrepancies.

Bronze has its own hygiene requirements, and this gets overlooked. "Faithful witness" does not mean "dump zone." The data lands in the producer’s format and the producer’s language, but it lands in a governed namespace with clear ownership, access controls, and retention policies. The zone itself is clean even if the data inside it is messy. Think of it as a well-organized evidence locker. The evidence might be chaotic — bloodstained clothing, handwritten notes, partial recordings — but the locker itself has a catalog number, a chain of custody, and a lock.

One final Bronze principle, and it makes the architecture rigid in the right places: intelligence is never driven from Bronze. You do not build dashboards off Bronze. You do not let agents query Bronze. You do not run analytics on Bronze. You always move through Silver. Bronze is the historical record, not the operational surface. This is not optional. If you let consumers query Bronze directly, you are serving raw chicken. Someone will get sick.

*Pattern: Bronze Ingestion Contract*

_Context:_ Raw data lands from external producers into the platform’s receiving zone.

_Problem:_ Without immutability, the accountability boundary between producer and consumer dissolves. Debugging becomes impossible because the evidence has been modified.

_Solution:_ Bronze is permanent, immutable, in the producer’s format and language. No cleaning, no transformation, no enrichment. Governed namespace with ownership, access controls, retention. Intelligence is never driven from Bronze — consumers always go through Silver. Producers own Bronze; the platform owns interpretation.

_Example:_ A vendor lands market data in a Bronze zone. When prices fail reconciliation, the team points to Bronze and says: "this is what you delivered." Argument resolved. Had the data been cleaned on ingest, the discrepancy would be unfindable.

_Consequences:_ Storage cost of permanent raw data at scale. Retention policy management adds governance overhead.

=== What Silver Actually Is

A pretense runs through the lakehouse era that needs naming. Medallion architecture has been marketed as a generational break from the data warehouse tradition. Vendor decks describe the Gold layer as "curated for consumption" without specifying the curation discipline, as if the modelling decisions previous generations agonized over have become matters of taste.

The opposite error is equally common: claiming that Silver is just Kimball with new labels, or that one tradition — dimensional modelling, enterprise data modelling — already solved the problem and the industry merely forgot the answer. Neither framing survives contact with what this architecture actually prescribes.

The warehouse traditions contributed principles that are real and durable. Grain discipline — one row per entity per event per time grain — is a property of how analytical computation works, not any single tradition’s invention. Kimball formalized it; the formalization was genuine; the principle predates and outlasts the formalization. Conformed dimensions — canonical shared definitions of Customer, Product, Account across business processes — remain the hardest problem in multi-domain data and the one most poorly solved by mesh-era vocabulary. SCD2 — closing a dimension row and opening a new one when a tracked attribute changes — is the only faithful way to answer "what was true at the time of this transaction?" These survive every technology migration because they describe properties of analytical reasoning, not properties of storage engines.

But the implementation patterns those traditions prescribed were built for constraints that no longer hold. Kimball designed star schemas for the _consumption_ layer — the thing users query — in an era of row-based engines where small dimension tables joined to large fact tables minimized I/O, and where the star schema itself served as self-documentation because no semantic layer existed. Inmon prescribed a monolithic enterprise model as the integration layer, centrally owned, in an era when centralized data teams were the only teams. Both assumed expensive storage, row-oriented engines, and human-only consumers.

What this architecture does is neither. It is a domain-canonical modelling layer built for columnar engines and AI consumers — two constraints that did not exist when either tradition was formalized.

Open the `marts/` directory of any mature transformation project. Count the `fct_` and `dim_` prefixes. Count the surrogate-key generation macros. Count the SCD2 materialization strategies. Practitioners are applying durable modelling principles under medallion branding, often without knowing where the principles came from. Every `fct_` prefix is the tell — grain discipline at the level of file naming, retained by engineers who absorbed the medallion vocabulary without the modelling vocabulary.

Grain survived the move to columnar engines because it is a property of how aggregations compose and how joins behave — not a property of where the bytes sit. The moment a practitioner builds `fct_orders`, they have committed to a grain, typically one row per order-line per event timestamp, and the rest of the downstream model arranges itself around that commitment. The lakehouse era did not eliminate the grain question. It eliminated the vocabulary for talking about it, with the result that grain mistakes are now made by accident rather than by argument.

*Pattern: Silver Grain Declaration*

_Context:_ Building fact tables in Silver.

_Problem:_ Without explicit grain, aggregations compose incorrectly, joins produce fan-outs, and downstream consumers silently double-count.

_Solution:_ Every fact table declares its grain explicitly in schema metadata: "one row per \{entity} per \{event} per \{time grain}." The grain statement is enforced by uniqueness tests. All downstream joins and aggregations are validated against the declared grain. Grain is a property of analytical computation, not of storage — it survives every technology migration.

_Example:_ `fct_executions` declares grain as one row per execution_id per event_timestamp. A downstream model that joins executions to a many-side dimension without an aggregation step fails the grain test and is blocked from deployment.

_Consequences:_ Grain enforcement blocks deployment of tables that violate it — including legitimate denormalized use cases that don’t fit the one-row-per-entity model.

Slowly changing dimensions tell the same story. SCD2 — the pattern in which a dimension row is closed and a new row opened when a tracked attribute changes, with `valid_from`, `valid_to`, and a current-flag — is presented in vendor marketing as having been superseded by table-level time travel on Delta or Iceberg. A category error. Table-level time travel answers the question "what did this table look like on Tuesday?" SCD2 answers a different question: "what was this customer’s segment at the time of this transaction?" Analysts and regulators ask the second question, and the answer must travel on the row of the dimension, not on the table-level history of the warehouse. Every mature lakehouse that touches analytics ends up materializing `valid_from` and `valid_to` columns, maintained by MERGE statements that implement the same row-level historicization pattern the warehouse literature formalized decades ago. The technology changed. The pattern did not.

*Pattern: Silver SCD2*

_Context:_ Dimension attributes change over time (customer segment, counterparty classification, instrument status).

_Problem:_ Overwriting dimension attributes destroys the ability to answer "what was true at the time of this transaction?" — the question analysts and regulators actually ask.

_Solution:_ Every tracked dimension attribute carries `valid_from`, `valid_to`, and a current-flag. When an attribute changes, the old row is closed (valid_to set) and a new row opens (new valid_from). The old row is never deleted. System time columns (`system_from`, `system_to`) record when the warehouse learned about each version, enabling bitemporal queries. SCD2 is implemented via MERGE or snapshot strategies in the transformation tool (dbt, Dataform, or equivalent).

_Example:_ A counterparty’s risk tier changes from "retail" to "institutional." The dimension table now has two rows: the old row (valid_to = change date) and the new row (valid_from = change date). A query asking "what was the counterparty’s tier when this trade was booked?" joins on valid time, not current state.

_Consequences:_ Tables grow 3-10x compared to current-state-only. Query complexity increases — two-axis temporal joins are harder to write and slower to execute. Most BI tools cannot issue bitemporal queries natively.

And the hardest problem — how multiple business processes agree on what a Customer, a Product, an Account is — is more acute under mesh-flavored lakehouses than it ever was under unified enterprise warehouses. Each domain is now structurally empowered to own its own entity definitions. "Data contracts on shared entities" is the current vocabulary — addressing the same coordination problem the conformed dimension pattern solved, but frequently with worse tooling and no memory of why the problem was hard the first time.

*Pattern: Conformed Dimension*

_Context:_ Multiple business processes reference the same entity (Customer, Instrument, Account).

_Problem:_ Two domains use the same word for what may or may not be the same thing. Force them onto one table and you risk fusing concepts that should stay distinct (a bottleneck, and silent wrong aggregations when the rules actually differ); leave them fully separate and cross-domain queries join incompatible definitions. The instinct — "elect one canonical owner and make everyone reference it" — recreates the central data team the mesh was built to abolish.

_Solution:_ A conformed dimension is not a competing model to the colimit — it is the *special case of a bridge* (see "Multi-Domain Composition") where two domains genuinely share a concept. The decision rule is the sharing rule: **if the domains' rules for the concept agree, collapse it into one shared sub-theory both reference — that shared sub-theory is the conformed dimension, co-owned through the bridge, not handed to one domain. If the rules disagree, do not conform: keep the concept domain-local on each side and join them with a bridge that documents exactly what maps, what is lost, and what is partial.** Conforming is the reward for genuine agreement, never a coordination tax imposed top-down.

_Example:_ Compliance's `Customer` and Trading's `Counterparty` agree on legal-entity identity (`legal_entity_id`) — same key, same notion of when two are equal. That shared identity *conforms*: it becomes the shared sub-theory both domains include, maintained jointly through the bridge. But Compliance's `kyc_sub_segment` and Trading's `desk_assignment` are *not* shared — they live above the shared sub-theory, in their home domains, and the bridge records that each is lost on the other side. So `legal_entity_id` is conformed; the rest stays local. One concept, split correctly, joined by a documented bridge — not one domain owning the others' view of the customer.

_Consequences:_ The sharing rule has teeth and they cut: a team that conforms two genuinely-conflicting concepts "to be consistent" gets a silently-wrong aggregation, and a team that refuses to conform a genuinely-shared one gets a join that should have been free. The judgment of *whether* rules truly agree is the work — and it is exactly the judgment the continuous verification mesh re-checks every scan (the commutativity check), so a conformance that drifts out of agreement raises a witness rather than rotting in place.

Here is the synthesis. Silver is the domain-canonical modelling layer — not Kimball’s star schema, not Inmon’s enterprise warehouse, but a synthesis built for columnar engines with these structural commitments: grain discipline (one row per entity per event per time grain), SCD2 with full bitemporality extending beyond what either tradition prescribed, conformed dimensions where domains share entities, domain ownership rather than central ownership, and full historicization where nothing is overwritten and everything is traceable.

Gold is denormalized publication — flat, wide, question-driven, materialized for fast consumption. This is the strong default for analytical workloads in columnar engines, not an absolute rule. The exceptions are real: when a workload genuinely needs graph traversal, a graph-oriented Gold model is appropriate. When shared dimensions serve dozens of fact tables, a lightweight star schema in Gold may reduce duplication. When ML feature engineering requires normalized inputs, feature stores (already named as sui generis in this architecture) handle the case. The default is flat. The exceptions require justification. And the semantic layer is required in every case.

Why flat as default? Because the economics of columnar engines invert the old trade-offs. In row-based engines (Oracle, SQL Server, the pre-2015 world), star schemas were optimal: small dimension tables joined to large fact tables minimized I/O, and normalization saved storage at real cost. In columnar warehouses, the economics reverse. Columnar compression makes denormalized columns nearly free to store — repeated values in a column compress to near-zero. Joins cost more, not less, because they require shuffling data across distributed nodes. Predicate pushdown on flat tables is more efficient than join elimination across multiple tables. And the self-documentation benefit that star schemas once provided — you could look at a fact table surrounded by dimensions and understand the structure — is now handled by the semantic layer, which provides logical navigation, entity relationships, and hierarchies without requiring physical joins.

This is not "denormalization is always better." It is: in columnar engines with a semantic layer, the storage benefit of normalization is eliminated by compression, the self-documentation benefit is handled by the semantic layer, and the join cost is real. Flat tables are the strong default. Name the exceptions and require justification for each.

Above it all, the semantic layer transcends any single ontological pattern. It can overlay star schemas, flat tables, news headline lists, event streams — whatever serves the domain. At the semantic layer, any ontology is valid locally, as long as it maps into the colimit through bridges that declare their losses. Good governance is not conformity to a single modelling pattern. It is colimit health: are the morphisms between domain ontologies explicit, honest, dated, and signed?

For teams building today, the practical consequence is immediate. Silver requires modelling skills: grain analysis, conformed dimension design, slowly changing dimension strategy, bitemporal reasoning. A whole cohort of lakehouse-era data engineers has never engaged with these disciplines. They need to learn — not because any single tradition owns the principles, but because the principles are load-bearing regardless of what you call them. The vocabulary was never optional. It was misplaced.

=== Silver: The Canonical Interpretation

If Bronze is sworn testimony, Silver is the court’s official interpretation. It is where raw data becomes canonical knowledge, expressed in your vocabulary, structured according to your domain model, and governed by your definitions.

Silver is where the craft lives. Building Silver well requires understanding the domain deeply enough to make interpretive decisions that hold up under scrutiny. What is a "trade" in your system? What is an "account"? What is the relationship between an "order" and an "execution"? These are not technical questions. They are ontological commitments — decisions about what exists in your domain and how those things relate to each other. Get them wrong and every downstream consumer — human and machine — inherits the error.

One principle organizes Silver: one table per logical data set, and each table belongs to exactly one domain. Silver is the full domain model. The complete, canonical representation of each domain entity, with every attribute that matters, historicized where history matters.

SCD Type 2 — slowly changing dimensions with history tracking — is not optional in Silver. If an entity changes over time, those changes must be preserved. A customer’s risk classification today might be different from what it was six months ago. A trading pair’s fee structure might have changed three times this quarter. If you overwrite the old values, you have destroyed knowledge. Silver preserves the trajectory.

Bitemporality is not binary. Three tiers reflect the operational reality. Tier 1 — full bitemporality with valid-time and system-time at row level — applies to entities that participate in financial reporting, regulatory submissions, or risk calculations: counterparties, positions, classifications, any entity a regulator might ask about. Tier 2 — SCD2 with valid-time history but without explicit system-time tracking — applies to entities that change meaningfully but are not audit-critical: desk assignments, strategy classifications, fee schedules. Tier 3 — current-state-only with audit logging — applies to stable reference data that changes rarely and predictably: currencies, countries, static instrument lookups. Applying Tier 1 universally wastes engineering effort, increases query complexity, and creates maintenance burden for tables where the temporal questions will never be asked. Document the tier assignment per entity. Review annually. Promote when regulatory requirements change.

What confuses people most: Silver is business-facing. Both Silver and Gold are. Silver is not "technical" and Gold is not "business." Both are business-facing. What distinguishes them is curation.

Silver is the full model. It contains everything. Every attribute, every relationship, every historical state. Gold is the curated answer — a specific selection from Silver, shaped to answer a specific set of business questions. Think of Silver as the complete recipe book and Gold as tonight’s menu. The recipe book is available to anyone who wants it. But when a customer sits down, you hand them the menu.

Now the architectural decision that matters most: the schema definition file IS the logical data model. In dbt this is `schema.yml`; the principle applies to any transformation framework where model metadata lives alongside model code. There is no separate LDM document. There is no Visio diagram that drifts from implementation. There is no wiki page maintained by someone who left the company two years ago. The schema file — the same file that defines tests, descriptions, and column metadata — is the logical data model. Change the model, change the documentation, because they are the same file.

Documentation drift is the default at every organization. You build a beautiful logical data model during the design phase. You implement it. Six months later, the implementation has diverged. A year later, the LDM is fiction. Two years later, nobody even knows the LDM exists.

Not a people problem — an architecture problem. If the documentation lives in a separate system from the implementation, it will drift. Not might drift. Will drift. The only documentation that survives is documentation that is embedded in the execution pipeline. The schema definition file is embedded in the execution pipeline. Every deployment reads it. Every test references it. Every downstream consumer inherits it. When you change a column, you change the description in the same file, in the same commit, reviewed by the same pull request. Drift is eliminated by construction.

Community consensus has caught up. If you view the transformation tool purely as a way to run SQL, you are missing half the picture. SQL handles the logic; the schema metadata handles the architecture. But most teams still treat the metadata as an afterthought — fill in a description if you have time, leave it blank if you do not. That is a governance failure hiding in plain sight.

Optimizing Silver — moving from five redundant copies of a trade table to a single canonical representation — saves significant money. But the funding comes from AI: conversational analytics, agent infrastructure, machine-readable data. Cost savings are a side effect of doing the semantics right, which means you do not have to choose between "save money" and "build for AI." Same workstream. Every dollar spent deduplicating Silver is simultaneously a dollar spent making your data machine-readable.

Consider the reverse. A finance team was running account reconciliation via Excel spreadsheets and macros — a single daily snapshot, manual processes that appeared to take hours. When diagnosed, the bottleneck was inherited technical debt: code hacked together years ago, never revisited, running on assumptions that were no longer true. Reconciliation could have run hourly. But because the data lived outside the medallion architecture — trapped in spreadsheets, driven off Bronze-equivalent sources without Silver interpretation — nobody had questioned the constraint. What looked like a fundamental limitation was just the absence of architecture. Move that knowledge into Silver, materialize the reconciliation logic in Gold, and the "hours-long process" becomes a table refresh.

=== Gold: The Knowledge Menu

Gold is where most data platforms fail, and they fail for a reason that seems paradoxical: they fail because Gold is easy to build and hard to govern.

Building a Gold table is simple. Take some Silver tables, join them together, denormalize the result, materialize it as a view or a table. Any competent data engineer can do this in an afternoon. And that is the problem. Because when building Gold is easy and governing Gold is hard, you end up with a hundred Gold tables that nobody catalogs, nobody describes, and nobody maintains. You end up with a data swamp.

Wall Street is populated with failed data lakes, and they failed in exactly this way. People denormalize into Gold, do not catalog, keep throwing in more facts, replicate without governance. Gold becomes garbage — not because the data is bad, but because nobody tracked what Gold tables exist, what questions they answer, what definitions they use, or how they relate to each other.


[#fig-data-swamp]
.Uncatalogued Gold becomes a swamp: hundreds of tables, nobody knows what half of them hold, nobody trusts the numbers.
image::figures/data-swamp.png[data swamp,width=600,align="center"]

Prevention is simple to state and hard to execute: each Gold table answers a specific set of business questions. Design starts from the question, not from the schema. You do not build a Gold table and then figure out who uses it. You identify the question — "What is our trading volume by product by day?" — and then you build the Gold table that answers it. Every column in the table exists because it contributes to answering that question. Every column has a description. Every table has an owner.

*Pattern: Question-Driven Gold*

_Context:_ Designing a new Gold table for analytical consumption.

_Problem:_ Gold tables designed from schemas rather than questions proliferate without purpose — phantom schemas that add maintenance burden without answering anything.

_Solution:_ Design starts from the business question: "What is our trading volume by product by day?" The Gold table is shaped to answer that question. Every column exists because it contributes to the answer. The table is flat and denormalized — no joins required at consumption time. It carries a documented business question in its schema metadata. If you cannot state the question, the table should not exist.

_Example:_ A stakeholder asks: "What is our daily P&L by desk by strategy?" Gold table `gold_pnl_daily_desk_strategy` is built with one row per desk per strategy per day, columns for mtm_pnl, realized_pnl, notional, trade_count. Every column described. Business question documented in schema metadata: "Daily P&L breakdown for desk-level strategy performance review."

_Consequences:_ Requires the business question to be articulated before building — blocks exploratory table creation that sometimes produces unexpected value.

A counterintuitive principle, until you have lived with the alternative: do not be minimal. When you build a Gold table, include original IDs alongside resolved names. Include the foreign keys alongside the denormalized attributes. Include the timestamps that let a consumer trace the data back to Silver and Bronze. Storage is cheap. Traceability is expensive. A Gold table that contains only the minimum columns needed for a specific report is a Gold table that cannot be audited, cannot be debugged, and cannot be extended without rebuilding.

Painful experience. At a previous institution, a team built beautifully minimal Gold tables — exactly the columns needed for the dashboard, nothing more. Six months later, a reconciliation failed. The team needed to trace a specific number back to its source. They could not, because the Gold table did not contain the IDs that would have made the trace possible. They had to rebuild the table, rerun the pipeline, and re-derive the number from Silver. The dashboard had been right all along. Proving it took three days instead of three minutes, because the Gold table had been optimized for elegance rather than traceability. Elegance you cannot audit is vanity.

Something mundane that matters enormously: business knowledge that lives in visualization tools rather than in governed data layers. Business users go to a finance lead’s Tableau views because "that’s where the insights live." Those insights are client-side widgets — derived calculations, conditional formatting, custom aggregations — all business knowledge trapped in a visualization tool. That knowledge is person-dependent, non-machine-readable, and fragile. If the person who built the Tableau workbook leaves, that knowledge is gone. It should reside in Gold. It should be a table, with a description, with an explore, accessible to any consumer including an AI agent. Moving business logic from visualization tools to governed Gold tables is a knowledge-rescue operation, not a technology project.

Now for the position that will upset people: Gold’s strong default is flat. Denormalized. No physical star schemas in Gold. No physical dimension tables. No joins required at consumption time.

This contradicts what most practitioners learned in school or on the job. The claim is precise: in columnar engines with a semantic layer, flat Gold tables are the strong default because the structural reasons for physical star schemas at the consumption layer have expired.

The columnar engine is the structural break. In row-based engines (Oracle, SQL Server, the pre-2015 world), star schemas were genuinely optimal at the consumption layer: small dimension tables joined to large fact tables minimized disk I/O, normalization saved storage at real cost, and the star schema itself served as self-documentation — you could look at a fact table surrounded by dimension tables and understand the structure without external tooling. The consumer was always human, a BI analyst writing SQL who could reason through joins.

In columnar engines, each of those conditions inverts. Columnar compression makes denormalized columns nearly free to store — repeated values in a column compress to near-zero, eliminating the storage argument for normalization. Joins cost more, not less, because they require shuffling data across distributed nodes; query plans on flat tables benefit from predicate pushdown and partition pruning that multi-table joins cannot exploit as efficiently. The semantic layer now provides the self-documenting function that star schemas once provided — logical navigation, entity relationships, hierarchies — without requiring physical joins. And the primary consumer is increasingly an AI agent, which burns tokens reasoning through joins and where hallucination risks multiply with every additional table the agent must discover and correlate.

When an AI agent answers a business question, it needs to identify the relevant tables, understand the join keys, construct the SQL, and execute the query. Every join is a decision point where the agent can go wrong. A flat Gold table eliminates those decision points. The agent reads one table, understands the columns from the semantic layer, and writes a simple query. Every join is a fork where the agent can take the wrong path, and "works most of the time" is not a standard anyone ships against.

An engineer presented a Gold model using a star schema — fact table, dimension tables, foreign keys. Textbook dimensional modelling, well-executed. My instinct was immediate: not star schema. Flat, denormalized. As we talked through the implications, the absurdity became clear. Silver was already denormalized along domain lines. The star schema was re-normalizing what Silver had already denormalized — taking apart a dish that the kitchen had already assembled, putting the ingredients back in separate containers, and asking the customer to reassemble them at the table. Why? Silver’s dimensional structure was already the correct analytical surface for dimensional exploration, exposed through the semantic layer. Re-normalizing it into a physical star schema in Gold duplicated structure that already existed.

That conversation led to a rewrite of our architectural standards and surfaced a deeper problem: our architecture document was ambiguous on this point. When another team member had their AI assistant read the old standards document, the AI concluded that star schemas were correct for Gold — because the document said so, implicitly, through its ambiguity. Machines do not resolve ambiguity through corridor conversation. If your architecture documents are not machine-readable and semantically precise, your AI consumers will inherit your confusion.

But flat Gold alone is not the answer. Gold operates in two modes, both governed by the same ontology. First, flat Gold tables: denormalized, question-driven materializations shipped for expedience, performance, and domain specialization — fast to build, fast to query, ideal for agents and dashboards. Second, Silver-as-Gold views: Silver tables exposed directly through the semantic layer for rich dimensional navigation — grain-defended, SCD2, conformed dimensions, serving analysts who drill, slice, explore, and run complex analytical queries. Both reachable through the same semantic layer. Both governed by the same vocabulary. The semantic layer is not a physical star schema — it is a logical ontology, a queryable web of concepts and relationships that encodes fact-to-dimension joins, hierarchies, and navigation paths without requiring physical re-materialization. Physical star schema is an implementation strategy. The semantic layer is ontological. Different categories entirely.

[#fig-silver-vs-gold]
.Silver (Dimensional) versus Gold (Denormalized) Structure
image::figures/silver-vs-gold.png[Silver (Dimensional) versus Gold (Denormalized) Structure,width=680,align=center]


Gold is the menu of what you want to talk to the agent about. A menu designed for any consumer — human or machine — who has a question about your business. Gold is codified business knowledge, accessible through a semantic layer that provides context, definitions, and navigational intelligence.

One more governance mechanism: every Gold model ships with an explore. An explore, in the generic sense, is a governed consumption interface — a structured, documented, access-controlled view of a Gold table that exposes it through the semantic layer. A Gold table without an explore is incomplete. It is an ungoverned flat file. The explore is infrastructure, not a nice-to-have feature. It is the thing that prevents Gold from degenerating into the swamp that killed every data lake on Wall Street.

No exceptions. Every Gold model ships with an explore. Building Gold is not just a technical task — it is a semantic task. You are not just writing a query and materializing a table. You are building a governed, described, navigable piece of business knowledge. Higher bar. Dramatically better result.

*Pattern: Explore Pairing*

_Context:_ A Gold table has been built and materialized.

_Problem:_ A Gold table without a semantic interface is an ungoverned flat file — queryable by anyone who knows it exists, with no definitions, no governed joins, no access controls at the semantic level.

_Solution:_ Every Gold table ships with a governed consumption interface (an "explore" in the generic sense): field definitions linked to the glossary, declared relationships to other entities, access controls, freshness signals, and navigational heuristics. The explore IS the table’s public interface. Building a Gold table without one is shipping code without tests — technically possible, architecturally incomplete.

_Example:_ `gold_pnl_daily_desk_strategy` ships with an explore that exposes: measures (mtm_pnl, realized_pnl as SUM), dimensions (desk_name, strategy_name, trade_date), a declared default time grain (daily), freshness metadata (last refresh timestamp), and access controls (finance + trading roles only).

_Consequences:_ Doubles the work of shipping a Gold table. Teams under delivery pressure will resist.

Sequencing matters. Teams routinely try to fix their Gold layer before defining the principles that should govern it. You cannot fix what you have not named. Define principles first, identify gaps, then refactor. Refactoring without standards is rearranging furniture in a room whose purpose nobody has agreed on.

Another anti-pattern: the phantom schema. A team builds a Gold table that replicates Silver’s structure with minor filtering or renaming — no real curation, no question answered, no semantic value added. Someone requested "a Gold version of X" without specifying what business question it should answer. Phantom schemas proliferate silently. Each one adds a table to the catalog, a model to the DAG, a maintenance burden to the team. None represent actual knowledge. Every Gold table must have a documented business question it answers. If you cannot state the question, the table should not exist.

One more category belongs near Gold but is not Gold: the feature store.
