ML feature stores look like Gold — wide, materialized, consumption-oriented — but they are a sui generis category in this architecture, and the position requires that they be treated as such. What defines a feature store is point-in-time correctness for training data. To train a supervised model correctly, the features available at label-assignment time must be reconstructible exactly — the feature values as the system would have known them at that moment, with no leakage from the future. This is bitemporality at row level, governed by training-time correctness rather than reporting-time auditability.

Feature stores therefore inherit Silver’s per-row bitemporal discipline but serve different consumers — training loops, online inference — and carry a different contract. They are not Gold and should not be governed as Gold. They get their own architectural category, their own temporal contract documentation, and their own clearly demarcated surface in the semantic layer. Why this matters: if feature stores are quietly governed as a kind of Gold, Gold’s clean table-level temporal contract is undermined, and the architecture devolves into a bitemporality-everywhere pattern that collapses the distinction between what was reported and what was true. Naming feature stores separately keeps both contracts clean.

*Pattern: Feature Store Contract*

_Context:_ ML models need training features with point-in-time correctness.

_Problem:_ If feature stores are governed as Gold, Gold’s clean table-level temporal contract is undermined. If governed as Silver, the consumption pattern (online inference, batch scoring) is misaligned.

_Solution:_ Feature stores are a sui generis architectural category — not Gold, not Silver. They inherit Silver’s row-level bitemporality but are governed by training-time correctness: features at label-assignment time must be reconstructible exactly, with no future leakage. They get their own surface in the semantic layer and their own contract documentation. They serve training loops and inference, not analytical queries.

_Example:_ `feature_store.counterparty_risk_features` carries row-level valid_from/valid_to and system_from/system_to. A training pipeline requesting features "as of March 15" gets exactly the feature values the system would have computed on March 15 — no corrections that arrived later, no attributes that changed after that date. The feature store’s contract is training-time correctness, distinct from Gold’s reporting-time auditability.

_Consequences:_ A third temporal contract alongside Silver and Gold adds cognitive and engineering overhead. Teams must understand three different bitemporal regimes.

=== Where Serious People Disagree

Real positions, held by serious people, that this architecture disagrees with for specific reasons.

*Star schemas in Gold.* The strongest counterargument deserves direct address. Joe Reis, whose work on data engineering fundamentals I respect, has argued that dismissing Kimball because of columnar storage is a dangerously ignorant statement. He is right — if the dismissal is based on columnar storage alone. Columnar storage does not make star schemas wrong. What makes star schemas the wrong _default_ in Gold is the conjunction of three conditions: columnar engines that invert the join/storage economics, a semantic layer that handles the self-documentation star schemas once provided, and AI consumers that pay a real cost for every join they must reason through. Remove any one of those three conditions and the argument weakens. In an environment without a semantic layer, star schemas still provide valuable self-documentation. In an environment without AI consumers, the join-reasoning cost is less acute. In a row-based environment, normalization still makes performance sense. But if all three conditions hold — and they increasingly do — then star schemas in Gold add complexity without benefit.

The warehouse traditions’ intellectual contributions are real and this architecture builds on them. Grain discipline — declaring the grain of every fact table explicitly — is the most important modelling principle the field has produced. Conformed dimensions are the ancestor of the shared vocabulary this architecture advocates. SCD2 is exactly what Silver implements, extended with full bitemporality. These were genuine contributions to a different era’s constraints. The principles endure. The implementation pattern — star schemas as the consumption layer — was optimized for row-based engines and human-only consumers, and neither condition holds as the default today.

Star schemas are not banned from the architecture. They live where the modelling discipline lives: in Silver. The semantic layer exposes Silver’s dimensional structure directly as governed analytical views when the consumption pattern requires it.

In semantic layer tools like LookML, every explore is a logical star schema — a base view joined to dimension views. The navigational and self-documenting benefits of dimensional modelling are preserved in the logical layer. What is eliminated is the physical re-materialization of that structure in Gold tables. The semantic layer encodes the dimensional model logically — fact-to-dimension relationships, hierarchies, navigation paths. Physical star schemas at the consumption layer duplicate structure that the semantic layer already provides.

*Collibra as separate governance overlay.* Data governance is a booming market — projected to reach nearly ten billion dollars by 2032. Collibra leads with enterprise compliance workflows, audit trails, and policy management. It serves heavily regulated industries where these capabilities are non-negotiable. So why argue against it?

Collibra as a tool is not the objection. Collibra as an architecture pattern is — governance living in a separate system, maintained by a separate team, connected to the execution pipeline by hope and good intentions. Large organizations accumulate metadata graveyards: ten thousand terms in a governance tool that nobody reads. Terms defined during a six-month project, delivered to applause, abandoned within a year — not because people were lazy, but because the architecture was wrong. If governance is not embedded in the execution pipeline, it drifts. If it drifts, it dies.

Tomorrow’s MDR is not a database you query to find out what data you have. It is context you populate into an agent’s reasoning window before it makes decisions — a briefing document that every operator reads before every mission, not a reference library nobody visits.

*Data mesh without a semantic core.* Data mesh correctly identifies that centralized data teams are bottlenecks. Average time to fulfill analytical requests has nearly doubled in the last five years. Domain ownership, data as product, federated governance — these are sound principles. Federated governance that works in practice: a small central team owns Silver and the semantic layer, while domain leads own their Gold models and their business definitions.

But data mesh as commonly implemented fails for a specific reason: it distributes ownership without distributing vocabulary. Each domain defines "trade" or "revenue" or "customer" in its own way. Cross-domain queries become impossible because the same word means different things in different domains. The result is not federated governance. It is Balkanized confusion.

A semantic layer is the missing infrastructure that makes data mesh workable. A shared vocabulary — a canonical glossary that all domains reference — is the unifying constraint that turns federation into coherence. Without it, data mesh is just a way to distribute your data problems to more people. Same problem, harder to see.

Even the founder of data mesh has acknowledged the gap. Execution has been hard, she says, and the concept has been reduced to data product management, with data products downgraded to nothing more than clean datasets with metadata. That is a damning self-assessment, and it confirms the structural point: clean datasets with metadata is not enough. You need a shared vocabulary — a semantic core — or the domains will diverge.

*Just add a catalog.* Enterprise reflex when the data swamp gets bad enough. Someone buys a catalog tool, a team spends six months cataloging tables, and the organization declares victory. But cataloging without a vocabulary is filing without an alphabet. If you catalog ten thousand tables with inconsistent names, conflicting definitions, and no ontological structure, you have a searchable mess, not a knowledge system. Cataloging is the packaging. Vocabulary is the content. You need both, and vocabulary comes first.

*Heavyweight ontology.* At the opposite end sit formal ontology projects — tens of thousands of terms, built over years by specialists, formally exquisite, operationally inert.

Quality is often extraordinary. But ontologies are not documents — they are living systems. And humans cannot maintain living systems at the scale required. These projects were designed to be maintained by human experts meeting periodically to review and update terms. At tens of thousands of terms, the maintenance burden exceeds human capacity. Terms drift. Relationships break. Ontology becomes monument.

Not less rigorous — differently rigorous. A lightweight vocabulary — anchored in ISO 11179 definitional discipline, scoped to the domains that matter, embedded in schema definition files — maintained continuously by AI. Precision without preciousness. Every definition meets the standard: precise, unambiguous, non-circular, stated in natural language, domain-scoped. But the maintenance is automated. The vocabulary lives in the execution pipeline, not in a separate document. And it scales because the maintenance cost is near zero.

=== The Seven Core Positions

Seven convictions, each hardened by failure, refined in arguments with smart people who disagreed.

{empty}1. *The Decoupling.* Physical storage and logical meaning are separate concerns. How data is stored on disk has nothing to do with how it is understood by consumers. A flat Gold table is physically a wide row. A Silver-as-Gold view is physically a dimensional model exposed through the semantic layer. Both are logically sets of related concepts connected by the ontology. Confusing physical and logical — building logical structure into physical layout, as star schemas do — is the category error that the industry has committed for decades.

{empty}2. *Upstream Always Wins.* Meaning flows in one direction: from the glossary, to the schema definition, to the semantic layer. If the glossary says "trading volume" means the total quantity of assets exchanged in a given period, then the schema describes it that way, and the semantic layer presents it that way. No tier may introduce its own definition. No downstream system may override an upstream meaning. Authoritarian by design. Vocabulary governance is not a democracy.

{empty}3. *Silver Is the Domain-Canonical Model.* Silver carries the modelling discipline — grain, history where history is asked for (temporal depth applied in tiers, not everywhere), shared concepts conformed through bridges where domains genuinely agree, domain ownership. Gold’s fast default is flat denormalized publication; Silver-as-Gold views provide dimensional depth. The semantic layer composes across both, and at the semantic layer, any local ontology is valid as long as it maps into the colimit through bridges that declare their losses. The architecture distributes concerns across layers: the modelling discipline lives in Silver, the publication simplicity lives in Gold, and the logical richness — entity relationships, hierarchies, navigational paths — lives in the semantic layer.

{empty}4. *AI as Consumer, Not Exception.* AI goes through the same semantic layer as every other consumer. Same governance, same definitions, same access controls. No "AI data layer." No shadow infrastructure. If the AI gets a different version of the data than the human, you have built a system that lies.

{empty}5. *Governance by Construction.* Governance that is not embedded in the execution pipeline does not exist. Naming conventions enforce semantics. Namespaces determine ownership. Mandatory explores prevent Gold from becoming a swamp. Manual governance decays. Constructed governance persists.

{empty}6. *Semantic Infrastructure as Prerequisite.* Without vocabulary, everyone is guessing. Conversational analytics at ninety-nine percent accuracy fails at the one percent mark because of missing context. Data mesh fails because domains do not share vocabulary. Agents fail because column names are ambiguous. Vocabulary is not the last mile. It is the first.

{empty}7. *Epistemic Honesty.* Data has freshness. Models have confidence intervals. Definitions have scope. When an agent answers a question using data that is twelve hours stale, it should say so. When a Gold table does not cover a particular time period, the query should acknowledge the gap. A system that presents uncertain answers with false confidence is worse than a system that says "I do not know."

None of these were deduced from first principles in a quiet room. Each was forged in an argument, a failure, or a near-miss. The Decoupling crystallized during a conversation where an engineer was re-normalizing Gold into a star schema — building logical structure into physical layout — and there was no document that explained why this was wrong. Upstream Always Wins emerged from the tenth time I watched a downstream team introduce their own definition of a term that the glossary had already defined, creating a contradiction that took weeks to resolve. Governance by Construction was born from the exhaustion of watching quarterly governance meetings approve changes to a data dictionary that nobody read.

These seven are not independent — they are load-bearing in combination. Pull one out and the others wobble.

=== Multi-Domain Composition: Where Architecture Meets Ontology

Everything so far operates within a single domain. Within one domain, the architecture is clean, well-understood, and operational.

But organizations do not live in single domains. A cryptocurrency exchange operates across at least seven: Pre-Trade, Trading, Custody, Post-Trade and Risk, Market Intelligence, Treasury, and Finance. Each of these domains has its own entities, its own relationships, its own vocabulary. A "trade" in the Trading domain is not the same concept as a "journal entry" in the Finance domain — but they are connected. A "wallet balance" in Custody is not the same thing as a "position" in Treasury — but they correspond. The hardest questions any organization asks are the ones that span these boundaries.

How do you compose domains? Most data architectures answer badly, or do not answer at all. The standard approach is ad-hoc: build a cross-domain mart by joining tables from different domains, resolve naming conflicts manually, hope for the best. Fragile, ungoverned, semantically incoherent. Every cross-domain query becomes a bespoke engineering project.

A conceptual upgrade replaces ad-hoc integration with principled composition — one that draws on mathematical structure.

Start with what a domain actually is. Each business domain, when properly architected, is a self-contained conceptual system. It has objects — the entities it tracks, like Trade, Position, Balance, Instrument, Market. It has relationships — the morphisms that connect those entities, like "a Trade belongs to an Instrument which belongs to a Market," or "a Position aggregates Trades over a time window." And those relationships compose: if a Trade belongs to an Instrument, and an Instrument belongs to a Market, then the Trade relates to a Market through that composition. The composition is associative — it does not matter whether you first resolve Trade-to-Instrument and then Instrument-to-Market, or whether you compose the entire path at once. The result is the same.

This structure has a precise mathematical name: a category — objects, morphisms (arrows between objects), associative composition, identity morphisms. The term sounds intimidating; the idea is not. Every well-designed domain model is already a category, whether the architect knows it or not. Gold tables and Silver-as-Gold views — the governed consumption surfaces — are the objects. Foreign-key relationships and semantic connections are the morphisms. Chaining relationships — Trade to Instrument to Market — is composition. Naming the structure does not change it. It makes it possible to reason about what happens when structures interact.

*Pattern: Domain Category*

_Context:_ A business domain has been modeled in Silver and Gold.

_Problem:_ Without formal structure, cross-domain integration is ad-hoc — engineers join tables by physical key and hope the semantics align.

_Solution:_ Each domain, when properly modeled, is a category: entities are objects, relationships between entities are morphisms, and chaining relationships is composition. The composition is associative — Trade to Instrument to Market yields the same result regardless of grouping. Naming this structure does not change it; it makes it possible to reason about what happens when domains interact. Every well-designed domain model is already a category, whether the architect knows it or not.

_Example:_ Trading domain: objects are Trade, Instrument, Market, Desk. Morphisms: "Trade executes on Instrument," "Instrument belongs to Market," "Trade is booked to Desk." Composition: Trade to Instrument to Market gives the market for any trade. The domain’s Gold tables are the objects; the joins and foreign keys are the morphisms.

_Consequences:_ Naming the structure is easy. Maintaining the composition laws as the domain evolves is not — morphisms break when entities are renamed or restructured.

Domains do not exist in isolation. Where they share concepts, explicit mappings connect them. A trade in the Trading domain becomes a journal entry trigger in the Finance domain. A wallet balance in Custody corresponds to a position in Treasury. A market price in Market Intelligence feeds a mark-to-market valuation in Post-Trade and Risk.

Cross-domain mappings are not ad-hoc joins. They are governed, documented, versioned structures with their own lineage and their own accountability — and critically, they preserve the relationships between entities. If two trades in the Trading domain relate to the same instrument, then their corresponding journal entries in the Finance domain should relate to the same financial product. Structure in, structure out.

This too has a precise name: a functor. A functor is a structure-preserving mapping between categories. It maps objects to objects and morphisms to morphisms, and it preserves composition — if two morphisms compose in the source category, their images compose in the target category. In concrete terms: the bridge between Trading and Finance maps entities to entities and relationships to relationships, respecting the internal logic of both domains. Not just "this trade maps to this journal entry" — "the relationship between trades and instruments maps to the relationship between journal entries and financial products."

Concretely: Trading has Trade, Instrument, and "Trade executes on Instrument." Finance has Journal Entry, Financial Product, and "Journal Entry references Financial Product." Because the bridge is a functor, it maps Trade to Journal Entry and Instrument to Financial Product, and also maps "executes on" to "references" — structural correspondence, not just row-level mapping.

*Pattern: Bridge Functor*

_Context:_ Two domains share concepts that need to be connected (Trading’s Trade and Finance’s Journal Entry).

_Problem:_ Ad-hoc joins between domain tables ignore structural relationships — matching rows by key without preserving the relationships between entities. After ten such joins, the enterprise has ten incompatible local mappings.

_Solution:_ A bridge functor maps objects to objects AND morphisms to morphisms between two domains, preserving composition. If Trade maps to Instrument in Trading, then JournalEntry maps to FinancialProduct in Finance, and the bridge maps both entities AND the relationship between them. The bridge is a governed, versioned artifact — not a one-off SQL join. It is documented, continuously verified, and maintained as part of the semantic layer: a verification agent re-reads it against the live state of both domains on every scan, so the bridge is known to hold now, not merely to have been approved once.

_Example:_ Trading-to-Finance bridge: Trade maps to JournalEntry, Instrument maps to FinancialProduct. The relationship "Trade executes on Instrument" maps to "JournalEntry references FinancialProduct." Structure in, structure out. When someone asks "revenue by instrument," the bridge guarantees that Trading’s instrument and Finance’s financial product are correctly aligned — not just key-matched but structurally correspondent.

_Consequences:_ Bridge maintenance scales combinatorially with domain count. At 20+ domains, bridge maintenance can exceed the capacity of a small central team.

Why this matters operationally: it determines what happens when someone asks a cross-domain question. When a finance analyst asks "What is the revenue impact of our top-traded instruments?", the system needs to compose two domains: Trading, where instruments and trade volumes live, and Finance, where revenue attribution lives. In a traditional architecture, an engineer would build a custom mart for this question — joining trading tables to finance tables, resolving naming conflicts, hardcoding the logic. In a compositional architecture, the system follows the bridges.

Trading’s Gold tables enter through the Trading-to-Finance bridge, which maps entities to entities and preserves relationships. Each domain’s entities retain their identity — a Trade is still a Trade, a Journal Entry is still a Journal Entry — but the composed view shows how they relate.

This composed view is a colimit — the universal construction that composes multiple perspectives into the smallest consistent whole. "Universal" here means that any other way of composing the same perspectives factors through the colimit — it is the canonical composition, the one that captures everything the perspectives share without introducing anything they do not. "Smallest consistent whole" means that it does not duplicate information and it does not introduce contradictions. If Trading and Finance both reference the same instrument, the colimit identifies them as the same entity, not two copies.

But here precision matters, because the colimit is easy to mistake for something it is not.

A traditional unified enterprise data model is a snapshot — one schema, frozen at one point in organizational time, claiming to represent every domain’s vocabulary in a single canonical structure. Information architects have been building these for decades. They take years to construct, they claim completeness, and they fail — not occasionally, but structurally, every time — because the enterprise does not hold still. Domains evolve. Acquisitions arrive. Regulations redefine terms. By the time the unified model is "complete" for the domains as they existed at project start, the domains have moved. The model is accurate about an organization that no longer exists.

The colimit described here is not that construction. It is a trajectory through organizational time, not a snapshot.

Every morphism in the colimit is dated. Every domain ontology is versioned. The colimit at time T — the composition of domain categories and bridge functors as they exist at T — is a different object from the colimit at time T+1, when a regulatory change has altered the Compliance domain’s entity definitions, or an acquisition has introduced a new domain entirely, or a product launch has created concepts that did not exist the previous quarter. The colimit is not built once and maintained. It evolves as the organization evolves, because the domains it composes are themselves evolving.

And the colimit has gaps — by design. Not every domain pair has a morphism, and not every concept is mapped. The gaps are deliberate, not failures. The unified enterprise model claims completeness and fails silently — the mapping from Trading to Market Intelligence is absent, but the model does not declare the absence. The colimit declares its gaps explicitly: Trading and Compliance are connected through this morphism, verified as of the last scan, with the date of that verification on its face. Trading and Market Intelligence are not yet connected. The gap is visible, not hidden. You work with local manifolds — the subset of domains you actually need for a given question — and you do not pretend the full union of all terms exists when it does not.

This means the colimit is not a deliverable. It is an eval target — a governance metric, like code coverage or test completeness, that you measure against and never reach 100%. "How healthy is your colimit?" is the governance question. Colimit health is measured by: completeness of morphisms (what percentage of domain pairs that should be connected are connected?), honesty of bridges (are losses and partialities documented?), coverage of shared concepts (are the terms that span domains actually mapped?), and staleness (when were morphisms last reviewed? are any overdue?). You track colimit health the way you track uptime — continuously, with alerts when it degrades, with a clear understanding that perfection is asymptotic.

A cross-domain mart, properly constructed, is not a bespoke engineering artifact. It is the colimit of the diagram formed by the relevant domain categories and their bridge functors. Two domains, three, or all seven — the construction is the same regardless of scale: identify the domains, follow the bridges, compose the perspectives, unify shared entities. Consistent by construction, not by manual reconciliation.

[#fig-colimit-composition]
.Domain Categories, Morphisms, and the Colimit
image::figures/colimit-composition.png[Domain Categories, Morphisms, and the Colimit,width=680,align=center]


But the construction is always local to the moment and local to the question — which domains, which morphisms, which versions of each.

*Pattern: Colimit Query*

_Context:_ A business question spans multiple domains ("What is the revenue impact of our top-traded instruments?").

_Problem:_ Without principled composition, cross-domain queries are bespoke engineering projects — each one a custom mart, each one making ad-hoc join decisions, each one ungoverned.

_Solution:_ The colimit is the universal composition of domain categories connected by bridge functors. It identifies shared entities (Trading’s Instrument and Finance’s FinancialProduct are the same entity, seen from two perspectives), preserves each domain’s internal structure, and produces the smallest consistent cross-domain view. Any cross-domain query factors through the colimit. The colimit is implemented as governed cross-domain mart tables with explicit lineage showing which domains contributed and through which bridges.

_Example:_ "Revenue per top-traded instrument" factors through the Trading-Finance colimit. The colimit unifies Instrument and FinancialProduct (same entity, two perspectives), preserves Trading’s volume metrics and Finance’s revenue attribution, and produces a cross-domain view where trade_count and revenue are correctly joined at the instrument level. The mart’s schema documents: domains involved (Trading, Finance), bridge model (trading_finance_bridge), entities unified (Instrument/FinancialProduct via legal_entity_id).

_Consequences:_ Cross-domain queries through the colimit are slower than ad-hoc joins. The governance overhead is justified only for questions asked repeatedly.

Most information architecture still operates in a first-order logic frame. Entities have attributes. Attributes have values. Queries are predicates that filter and aggregate over those values. Joins are predicates that match rows across tables. Entirely predicative — asserting conditions and checking whether data satisfies them.

Category theory replaces predication with composition as the primitive operation. Instead of asking "which rows in table A match which rows in table B on this key?", you ask "how does the structure of domain A map into the structure of domain B, and what is the universal composition of both?" One gives you a result set. The other gives you a governed, structure-preserving, identity-respecting integration. One is a query. The other is architecture.

Without this upgrade, every cross-domain question is a bespoke integration project. With it, composition follows from the domain models and their bridges — governed artifacts that are documented, versioned, and maintained as part of the semantic layer.

In practice, implementation maps onto existing tools. Each domain’s Gold tables form an independent collection — a self-contained set of entities and relationships, exposed through its own semantic model and explore. Each is internally consistent, governed by its own vocabulary, documented in its own schema definition file.

Cross-domain mart tables are explicit compositions. The transformation tool’s lineage shows which domain Gold tables contributed and through which bridge models the data flowed. The mart’s schema definition documents the composition — domains involved, bridge models governing the mapping, entities unified. Documentation is not an afterthought; it is the composition itself, made visible.

The semantic layer manages composition at the consumption level. An explore that spans domains declares its domain dependencies explicitly. When an explore references both Trading Gold tables and Finance Gold tables, the semantic model file documents the join logic, the relationship cardinality, and the semantic rules for resolving polysemous terms. A "trade" in a cross-domain explore is explicitly the Trading domain’s definition of "trade," not the Finance domain’s. Ambiguity — the thing that kills cross-domain queries in ad-hoc architectures — is eliminated at the semantic layer.

Metrics compose across domain boundaries in the same way. A metric like "revenue per traded instrument" draws from both the Finance domain (revenue) and the Trading domain (instrument, trade count). The metric definition declares its domain dependencies, its bridge models, and its composition logic. Not a standalone calculation — a composed measurement, a colimit of the Finance and Trading perspectives on the same underlying business activity.

One more dimension connects to operational governance: privilege-based domain expansion. A user starts in their home domain. A finance analyst opens the system and lands in the Finance domain — Finance Gold tables, Finance semantic layer, Finance vocabulary. They ask about revenue. The agent loads Finance context and answers from Finance Gold.

Then they ask: "How does this relate to our trading volume?" At this point, the system checks whether the analyst has access to the Trading domain. If yes, the view widens. The agent loads the Trading-to-Finance bridge, composes the two domain perspectives, and answers from the resulting cross-domain view. If no, the system explains what it cannot access and why. This is epistemic honesty applied to domain boundaries, not a failure. The system does not pretend the Trading domain does not exist. It says: "That question requires Trading domain access, which your role does not include. Contact your domain administrator."

Domain expansion is categorical in the precise sense. Adding a domain to the user’s view means adding a category to the diagram and recomputing the colimit. The agent does not re-query everything from scratch. It extends the existing view through the relevant bridge functor. Incremental and governed. Each expansion is an explicit architectural decision — which domain, through which bridge, with which access controls — not an ad-hoc join.

*Pattern: Privilege-Based Domain Expansion*

_Context:_ A user or agent is querying data and their question crosses a domain boundary.

_Problem:_ Without access-controlled domain expansion, either the agent silently pulls from domains the user should not see, or domain boundaries become invisible and ungoverned.

_Solution:_ Users start in their home domain. When a question requires a second domain, the system checks access rights before expanding the view. If authorized, the agent loads the bridge functor and composes the two domain perspectives. If not authorized, the system states explicitly what it cannot access and why. Each domain expansion is an explicit architectural decision — which domain, through which bridge, with which access controls. Adding a domain means adding a category to the diagram and extending the colimit incrementally.

_Example:_ A finance analyst asks about revenue (Finance domain — authorized). Then asks: "How does this relate to our trading volume?" System checks Trading domain access. Authorized: loads Trading-Finance bridge, composes perspectives, answers from the cross-domain view. Not authorized: "That question requires Trading domain access, which your role does not include. Contact your domain administrator."

_Consequences:_ Incremental domain loading adds latency. Users accustomed to seeing all data at once experience the access checks as friction.

Categorical composition resolves the tension that data mesh identified but could not solve. Data mesh correctly argued that domains should own their data. Domain ownership without a composition mechanism produces silos. Categories preserve domain ownership — each domain is a self-contained category with its own governance — while providing a principled, governed mechanism for cross-domain composition. Domains remain autonomous. Composition is explicit. Shared vocabulary is not imposed from above; it emerges from the bridges, co-owned by the domains they connect.

Most data architecture treats integration as plumbing — connect the pipes, match the keys, reconcile the conflicts. Category theory reveals it as a structural problem: how do you compose conceptual systems while preserving the identity and coherence of each? Glue them by pushout along what they share. The static mathematics — how to compose specifications without contradiction — was settled in the specification-composition tradition decades ago. What remains, and what this book is really about, is the part that tradition did not face: composing systems that will not hold still, and using AI to keep the gluing current. That, plus the organizational will to treat cross-domain composition as architecture rather than ad-hoc query logic.

==== The Colimit Is Not a Unified Enterprise Model

A skeptic will say: this is a unified enterprise data model under different vocabulary. The objection deserves a direct answer, because the difference is structural, not cosmetic.

A unified enterprise data model is a noun — a thing you build and deliver. It is a single schema, constructed at one point in time, that claims to capture every domain’s vocabulary in one canonical structure. Completeness is required: if a domain is not in the model, the model is incomplete. The model is static: once delivered, it must be manually maintained against organizational change. No organization has ever successfully delivered one at enterprise scale. The projects take years, the domains evolve faster than the model can be updated, and the result is either abandoned or maintained as a regulatory fiction that no operational system trusts.

The colimit is a verb — something you track, measure, and use AI to keep current. It is not a single schema but a composition of local schemas connected by explicit, versioned, gap-aware morphisms. Completeness is not required: the colimit at any moment declares what is connected and what is not. Gaps are features, not failures — they tell you where governance work remains. The colimit evolves as domains evolve, because every constituent category and every morphism carries a version and a date. The colimit at time T is a different mathematical object from the colimit at time T+1, and the system tracks the difference.

The unified model asks: have we built it? The answer is always "not yet" or "yes, but it is out of date." The colimit asks: how healthy is it? The answer is a score — morphism completeness, bridge honesty, concept coverage, staleness — and the score changes as the organization changes. One is a deliverable that never arrives. The other is a practice that persists. The difference is not vocabulary but the gap between a product that ships and a practice that endures.

==== Morphism Honesty

A colimit without honest morphisms is unification with extra ceremony. Morphism honesty is unglamorous, painstaking work — and it is what turns the colimit from a framework into engineering.

An honest morphism between two domain entities is not a field mapping. It is a contract between two ways of knowing. It is an explicit, named, dated piece of documentation — signed by both domains — that states what the source entity maps to in the target, what attributes survive the map, what attributes are lost, what attributes the target adds that the source does not constrain, where the map is partial on either side, what the most recent verification scan found, and when that scan ran. Each of these is named explicitly. The losses and partialities are named explicitly. Nothing is implicit.

Consider, concretely, the morphism from Compliance.Customer to Trading.Counterparty. The map is well-defined for institutional clients onboarded through the compliance KYC process: `Compliance.Customer.legal_entity_id` is the source key, `Trading.Counterparty.cpty_id` carries the foreign key, the relationship is one-to-many because a single legal entity may have multiple trading accounts. So far this looks like a field mapping. It is not, because the interesting information is in what does not survive.

What does the map lose? `Compliance.Customer.kyc_sub_segment`, which has no Trading.Counterparty counterpart — forcing KYC sub-segmentation through would corrupt the trading model with attributes that have no operational meaning on that side. Where is the map partial? certain trading counterparties — proprietary desks, market-making subsidiaries — do not have a Compliance.Customer antecedent because they are internal entities that were never onboarded through KYC. Verified continuously rather than attested periodically: a verification agent re-checks the map against the current state of both domains on every scan — that the mapped keys still join, that no new attribute has appeared on either side that the map fails to account for, that the documented losses are still the actual losses. The map carries the date it was last confirmed to hold, which is last night, not the date a regulator last forced someone to look.

Every morphism looks like this when done honestly. Losses are documented because they are where the architecture is most likely to break under cross-domain queries. An agent traversing from a compliance question to a trading question reads this morphism and knows exactly what survived the map, what was lost, and where the gaps are. No inference required, no hallucination of relationships. Maps are first-class objects, and their honesty is what makes the colimit trustworthy rather than ceremonial.

*Pattern: Morphism Honesty*

_Context:_ A bridge functor connects two domains.

_Problem:_ If losses and partialities in the mapping are undocumented, cross-domain queries silently drop data or conflate concepts. The morphism looks complete but is not.

_Solution:_ Every morphism is explicit, named, versioned, and continuously verified. It documents: what attributes survive the map, what attributes are lost (and why), where the map is partial (entities in one domain with no counterpart in the other), what the last verification scan found, and when that scan ran. Losses and partialities are named explicitly. Nothing is implicit. The attestation is performed by each domain’s agent — which can read the map and the data it claims to align — with a human owner escalated only for the exceptions the agent cannot resolve. The morphism is a contract between two ways of knowing, kept honest by continuous verification rather than by signature.

_Example:_ Compliance.Customer to Trading.Counterparty. Survives: legal_entity_id to cpty_id, regulatory_classification. Lost: kyc_sub_segment (no trading analogue — forcing it through would corrupt the trading model). Partial: proprietary desks have no Compliance.Customer antecedent (internal entities, never KYC-onboarded). Verified: each domain’s agent, on the nightly scan. Last confirmed to hold: last night. The MiCA reclassification was caught by the scan that ran the morning after the rule changed the Compliance partition — not surfaced at a quarterly review a season later.

_Consequences:_ Documenting losses requires both domains to participate. One-sided morphisms are common in practice — the target domain sees no benefit in documenting losses.

==== Bitemporal Ontology

An axis the information architecture tradition has been almost entirely silent on, and it turns out to be load-bearing: ontologies themselves change over time. Meaning is not static. It shifts under regulatory pressure, organizational restructuring, market evolution, and the slow drift of institutional practice.

Compliance’s Customer definition in 2026 is not Compliance’s Customer definition in 2024. MiCA changed it, materially — the regulatory classification of certain crypto-asset service providers altered the segmentation logic, the jurisdiction tags, the onboarding requirements. The compliance-to-trading morphism in 2026 is therefore not the morphism that held in 2024, because the source object on one side of the map has shifted. An agent reading 2024 compliance documentation against 2026 trading data, with whatever morphism happens to be current, will produce confidently wrong answers about historical states of the world.

Bitemporality’s discipline — two-axis as-of queries, audit trails through versioned artifacts — applies in full to the ontology layer. Each domain ontology is itself a bitemporal object, with a valid time (when the definition applied in the world) and a system time (when it was written down). Each morphism is bitemporal: it has a date of last review and it is keyed against the versions of the source and target ontologies it connects. A historical query requires the historical ontology and the historical morphisms, not the ones current today.

Fractal discipline. Bitemporality runs through the data, through the ontology, through the morphisms between ontologies. An acquisition changes domain definitions. A regulatory inflection reclassifies entities. A product launch creates concepts that did not exist the previous quarter. At every level where meaning is claimed, the two-axis temporal discipline applies. In regulated industries — every compliance reconstruction, every regulatory inquiry, every restated filing — answering "what did this term mean at that time, and what morphism connected it to that other domain’s term at that time" is operationally non-negotiable.

==== Finishability

Colimit architecture makes the work finishable because it never requires the whole to be complete before the parts are useful.

Each domain ontology can be captured, documented, and served while morphisms to other domains are still being written. Each morphism is useful the moment it is signed — an agent or analyst working across two domains sees the two local ontologies plus the explicit morphism between them, regardless of how many other morphisms across the enterprise remain absent. The agent working across more domains than have been mapped sees the gap, transparently, and can request that the next morphism be written.

Contrast this with the information architecture tradition’s "unified enterprise model" dream, which was intrinsically unfinishable. Everyone in the industry knows that information architecture "takes forever" and never ships. The observation is accurate. The usual explanation — scope creep, organizational politics, insufficient funding — is wrong. The failure is structural.

Information architects were trying to build a colimit. They did not call it that, but the aspiration — one model that captures all domain vocabularies and their relationships, with a canonical resolution for every shared concept — is exactly the colimit construction. It took forever because a colimit over a moving target cannot be computed manually. The enterprise keeps changing: domains evolve, acquisitions arrive, regulations redefine entity boundaries, products launch and retire. Manual maintenance cannot keep pace. By the time the logical data model was "complete" for the domains as they existed at project start, the domains had moved. The model was accurate about an organization that no longer existed. Multi-year LDM projects never shipped because they were trying to manually compute a colimit over a target that would not hold still, and the mathematics of the situation — the colimit changes when any constituent category or morphism changes — made the aspiration structurally unreachable by manual means.

This is not a criticism of information architects. The discipline was intellectually rigorous. The goal was correct. The maintenance model was lethal. No team of human architects, however skilled, can continuously recompute a colimit across seven evolving domains, each with dozens of entities, connected by morphisms that shift under regulatory and organizational pressure. The work exceeded human bandwidth, and the projects died — not from laziness but from impossibility.

AI changes the maintenance equation. Not by building the colimit once, but by making it continuously re-evaluable. AI-maintained morphisms: when a domain ontology changes, AI identifies which morphisms are affected and flags them for review. AI-detected gaps: when a cross-domain query fails because no morphism connects the relevant domains, the failure is signal — it tells you where to write the next bridge. AI-surfaced staleness: when was the Compliance-to-Trading morphism last reviewed? Is it still accurate against the current versions of both domain ontologies? The colimit becomes "sufficiently synthesized and immediately available" — not as a completed artifact that someone delivered, but as a living, continuously evaluated measure of governance health. What killed every information architecture project in the pre-AI era — the maintenance burden of keeping a global model current over evolving domains — is precisely what AI absorbs.

The colimit is finished, locally, when a local ontology is captured and the morphisms to its neighbors are written down. No part needs global completeness before it is in production and useful. Traditional IA failed not through lack of effort or competence, but because its axioms demanded global completeness before any part was useful, and its maintenance model could not keep pace with organizational evolution. Colimit architecture removes both constraints: locality makes parts useful immediately, and AI makes maintenance tractable.

==== The Semantic Layer as Sole Interface

Every commitment in this architecture — bitemporality, colimit, morphism honesty, documentation — is delivered through the semantic layer or through nothing.

A consumer who bypasses the semantic layer bypasses the bitemporal contracts. They conflate the two structurally distinct questions that bitemporality was built to keep separate — what was true, and what was reported — and they will get plausible answers without knowing which question they have asked. Without the layer, there is no contract.

A consumer who joins Silver tables directly bypasses the morphisms entirely. They compose by physical key and reinvent — badly, locally, inconsistently — the maps the colimit framework was built to make explicit. After ten such joins, the enterprise has ten incompatible local re-implementations of the same morphism, each with different lossiness, none signed. The colimit dissolves into silos.

[#fig-sole-interface]
.The Semantic Layer as Sole Interface
image::figures/sole-interface.png[The Semantic Layer as Sole Interface,width=680,align=center]


A consumer who queries direct SQL has access to column names and not much else. They are reasoning over the worst possible representation of the data — the representation the entire documentation discipline was written to argue against. Morphism narrative, edge-case warnings, temporal caveats: none of it is available below the semantic layer. The underlying database has no essay.

Not separable. Delivered together through one surface or through none. The semantic layer is the interface — not one among several — and nothing reaches the consumer except through it. Every gap an agent or analyst surfaces is a gap in the semantic layer, not a case for direct access. Remediation is documentation, ontology, morphism, or measure work — never a bypass.

Unpopular in the short term, correct in every term, because the alternative is slow fragmentation of every architectural commitment described above.

==== Rapid Onboarding: From Island to Archipelago

A trading desk needs a new analytical surface: executed volume classified by spread tier, bucketed by distance from mid price at execution time. The data exists in Silver — trade executions in one domain, market snapshots in another. A competent engineer can build a flat Gold table that answers the question in a few days. Denormalized, question-driven, shipped with an explore. Pattern followed. Question answered.

The table is an island.

Next month, the same desk asks: how does spread-tier distribution correlate with our daily P&L by strategy? That question spans two domains — the new Gold table lives in Market Intelligence (or Sales, or Pricing — the domain name is debatable, which is itself diagnostic); P&L lives in Finance. Without a morphism between them, the question becomes another bespoke engineering project. Another month. Another island.

Month after that: which counterparties are driving volume in the tightest spread tiers? Now the question touches Compliance (counterparty identity, KYC classification). No morphism. Another project. Another island.

Each island answers one question well. None of them compose. The organization accumulates Gold tables the way it accumulated dashboards — each one locally correct, collectively incoherent, and every cross-cutting question a fresh engineering engagement.

The colimit architecture changes this sequence. Building the Gold table is step one — necessary, fast, unchanged. Step two is the one most teams skip: register the new table’s concepts in the domain ontology. ``Spread tier'' is now a named concept in the Market Intelligence register, with a definition, a grain, a documented relationship to the mid-price proxy and the temporal window convention. ``Executed volume'' maps to the Trading domain’s execution grain. The morphism between them — the -second window join that aligns executions to snapshots — is a named, dated, signed bridge between two domain categories.

With these two steps done, the table is no longer an island. It is a node in an archipelago. The follow-up questions compose:

The P&L correlation question factors through the existing Trading-to-Finance morphism. The new Gold table plugs into it because its concepts are registered in Trading’s ontology, and Trading already has a bridge to Finance. The agent follows the morphisms: spread tier (Market Intelligence) latexmath:[$\to$] execution (Trading) latexmath:[$\to$] P&L (Finance). Each hop is a documented, governed bridge. No bespoke engineering.

The counterparty question factors through Trading-to-Compliance. Same mechanism. The agent reads the morphism, knows what survives the map (counterparty identity) and what is lost (KYC sub-segments), and composes the cross-domain view. Hours, not months.

Colimit health is what determines how fast this composition works. If the Trading-to-Finance morphism already exists (because someone wrote it for a previous cross-domain question), the new Gold table benefits immediately. If it does not exist, the new question surfaces a gap — and the gap becomes morphism work, not a bespoke Gold table. Each morphism written makes the next cross-domain question cheaper. The architecture accumulates connective tissue.

This is the practical payoff of treating the colimit as a living eval target. Not governance for its own sake. Speed. The ability to answer the next question without starting from scratch. An organization with twenty Gold tables and no morphisms has twenty islands. An organization with twenty Gold tables and fifteen morphisms has a navigable knowledge architecture where an agent can traverse from any domain to any connected domain, composing answers from governed bridges. The difference between the two is not the Gold tables — it is the gluing.

The team that ships Gold tables without morphisms will build fast and compose never. The team that writes the morphism alongside the Gold table — even a partial morphism, even one that documents losses and gaps — ships at the same speed and compounds its investment with every subsequent question. Morphisms are the compound interest of data architecture.

==== Where This Architecture Breaks

*Semantic layer as bottleneck.* When the extension intake slips from days to weeks, analysts route around it. They write direct SQL. They build local spreadsheets. Every workaround is a fork. The sole-interface commitment is the first casualty, and it dies quietly — not through a policy reversal but through accumulating exceptions that nobody tracks. The architecture described above assumes the semantic layer team can absorb new measure and dimension requests faster than the business generates them. When it cannot, the architecture fragments regardless of how sound the design is. Staffing the intake is not a nice-to-have. It is a structural prerequisite.

*Bitemporality storage cost.* Full SCD2 on every Silver dimension, at scale, produces tables three to ten times larger than non-bitemporal equivalents. At petabyte scale, this is a real budget conversation — tens of thousands of dollars per month in additional storage and query cost. The architectural argument for bitemporality is correct: you need both timelines or you cannot answer regulatory questions. But the cost is not zero, and teams that adopt full bitemporality without modeling the storage implications will face uncomfortable conversations with finance. Name the cost. Budget for it. Apply bitemporality in tiers: Tier 1 for regulated entities, Tier 2 for volatile dimensions, Tier 3 for stable reference data. Universal Tier 1 is the aspiration. Tiered adoption is the practice.

*Colimit scaling.* With seven domains and fifteen morphisms, colimit health is manageable. One team can review every cross-domain mapping quarterly. With two hundred domains, the combinatorial surface of cross-domain mappings exceeds any team’s capacity. The colimit is most useful in the five-to-thirty domain range. Beyond that, you need hierarchical composition — colimits of colimits, where clusters of related domains form sub-colimits that are then composed at a higher level — or you accept that most domain pairs will never be directly connected. This is not a failure of the theory. It is a scaling constraint that the theory does not advertise.

=== The Metadata Repository of the Future

Every organization has a metadata problem. They have too much data, too little context, and no reliable way to connect the two. The traditional solution is a metadata repository — a database that catalogs your data assets, tracks lineage, stores definitions, and provides a search interface. Collibra, Alation, and their competitors have built a multi-billion-dollar industry on this need.

Traditional metadata repositories solve the wrong problem. They answer the question: "What data do we have?" Useful, but the wrong starting point. Right question: "What does an agent need to know before it reasons over this data?"

Library catalog versus mission briefing. One tells you what books exist; the other tells you what you need to know to accomplish a specific objective.

Concretely: when an AI agent is about to answer a question about trading volume, it needs context — not just the schema of the Gold table. It needs to know: What does "trading volume" mean in this organization? What is included and what is excluded? What is the grain of the data? How fresh is it? What known quality issues exist? What related concepts should it be aware of? What caveats apply to this particular time period?

In the traditional MDR model, this context lives in a governance tool that the agent would need to query before answering — adding latency, complexity, and a dependency on a system that may or may not be available. In the model being built here, this context is populated into the agent’s reasoning window as a pre-flight briefing. Before the agent touches the data, it reads the relevant domain ontology, the ISO 11179-compliant definitions, the quality signals, the freshness metadata, and the tutorial-quality documentation that explains how the domain works.

Briefing composition is specific. Domain ontologies — not heavyweight OWL, but lightweight, machine-readable descriptions of concepts and their relationships. It includes definitional discipline drawn from ISO 11179 — the international standard for metadata registries that has been around since the 1990s, adopted successfully in public-sector metadata programs across multiple countries, but never operationalized at scale in commercial enterprise because humans could not maintain the registry. AI changes this equation. What was too expensive to maintain manually becomes cheap when AI does the maintenance.

And something traditional MDR vendors have never thought to include: tutorial-quality documentation that agents read as narrative context. "This domain covers market-making activity. A market maker provides liquidity by maintaining buy and sell orders. The key metrics are spread, fill rate, and inventory position. Spread is measured in basis points. Fill rate is the percentage of orders that result in trades. Inventory position can be positive (long) or negative (short)." An agent that reads this paragraph before querying the data will produce dramatically better results than an agent that reads only column names and type definitions.

Agents do not passively absorb this context. They perform pre-flight checks: read the quality signal — is this data set healthy, degraded, or failing? Check freshness — when was this data last updated, and is that recent enough for the question being asked? Verify access — does the requesting user have permission to see this data? If the data is stale, the agent says so. If access is restricted, the agent says so. This is epistemic honesty built into architecture, not bolted on as an afterthought.

Consider what this means in practice. A senior executive asks: "What was our total trading volume last month?" In the traditional model, this question goes to an analyst, who queries a dashboard, which queries a table, which may or may not be current, documented, or correctly defined. The analyst knows the caveats — this table excludes test accounts, it has a two-hour lag, the definition of "volume" changed in March — but those caveats live in the analyst’s head. If a different analyst answers the same question, they might use a different table with different caveats and get a different number.

In the MDR-as-context-engine model, the agent reads the pre-flight briefing before answering. It knows that "trading volume" means the total quantity of assets exchanged, excluding test accounts. It knows the data was last refreshed two hours ago. It knows that the definition changed in March and can flag this if the question spans that boundary. The answer comes with its own audit trail — not because someone built an audit feature, but because the context engine populated the relevant metadata into the agent’s reasoning window, and the agent included it in its response. The caveats are not in anyone’s head. They are in the architecture.

Catalog wars become relevant here. Data catalog market: projected to reach nearly ten billion dollars by 2032. Gartner returned to the Metadata Management Magic Quadrant in 2025 after a five-year hiatus, with a framing that captures the moment perfectly: "no metadata, no AI." Genuine need. But most catalog implementations are still oriented toward the old interaction model — humans browsing for data assets, discovering tables, reading descriptions. Catalogs that serve agents will win. Catalogs that serve analysts will become compliance artifacts.

This vision is not fully operational today. The knowledge catalog being built has limitations: lineage retention is measured in days, not months. IAM integration is incomplete. The agent pre-flight pattern is a design target that works in specific flows but is not yet generalized across all consumption paths. What exists is a detailed specification, a working prototype in key domains, and a clear architectural trajectory. What does not yet exist is a production system that does all of this at scale.

But trajectory matters more than current state. Browse-based MDR is a dead end — not because it is badly built, but because agents do not browse. They read context, reason over data, and produce answers. Tomorrow’s MDR is a context engine: it assembles the relevant context for a specific query, populates it into the agent’s reasoning window, and lets the agent do what agents do best.

ISO 11179 is the forgotten ancestor of everything described in this essay. Around since the 1990s. Its core insight — that a Data Element combines a semantic meaning with a representation, and that these two aspects should be managed separately — is exactly the Decoupling thesis. The standard has been adopted successfully in public-sector metadata programs in Australia, Canada, the United Kingdom, and the United States. It has been almost entirely ignored by commercial enterprise. Why? Because humans could not maintain the registry at scale. Right architecture, lethal maintenance assumption. AI eliminates it. ISO 11179 definitional discipline plus AI-powered maintenance is what makes the MDR-as-context-engine viable. The standard was always right. The maintenance model was always wrong. Now it can be fixed.

Four components form the knowledge architecture: Bronze (faithful witness), Silver (canonical interpretation), Gold (knowledge menu), and the MDR (context engine). Not a data platform with metadata sprinkled on top — an integrated system where data, meaning, and context are managed as a single concern.

Bronze is your supply chain. Silver is your kitchen. Gold is your menu. The MDR is your maitre d’ — the intelligence that knows what is on the menu tonight, what is fresh, and what the customer should know before ordering. Most data platforms today are warehouses with loading docks, and the restaurant described here is harder to build.

But the forcing function has arrived. Your primary consumer is no longer a human analyst who can squint at a messy dashboard and figure it out. It is an AI agent that needs clean semantics or it hallucinates. It cannot compensate for missing definitions, navigate undocumented star schemas, or reason through ambiguous column names. It needs a menu — or it produces garbage with confidence.

AI demands the architecture, and AI makes it viable. The maintenance burden that killed every formal ontology project, every metadata graveyard, every governance initiative that relied on human vigilance — AI absorbs it.

Most of this architecture is operational, with the rest under detailed specification. The seven positions are production standards, encoded in machine-readable documents, enforced by CI/CD pipelines, and consumed by the agents whose existence motivated the architecture in the first place.

The restaurant is open. The question for every data platform leader is whether they are building one or still managing a loading dock. The new customers do not rummage through ingredients. They read the menu — and the menu had better be right, because these customers will repeat your mistakes at scale without a flicker of embarrassment.

== Exploring the Glued Ontology

The flat Gold table answers the question it was built for, brilliantly and cheaply, and not one question more. That is its virtue and its limit. The analyst who asks "daily P&L by desk by strategy" gets a perfect answer; the analyst who then asks "...and how does that break down by the counterparty's custody region, for counterparties onboarded in the last year?" has left the table behind. The second question crosses three domains — Trading, Compliance, Custody — and no single flat table was built for it, because nobody anticipated it. This is not a defect to engineer away. It is the permanent condition of analysis: the next question is not on the menu.

The book has so far given two consumption surfaces. Flat Gold, for the anticipated question. And Silver-as-Gold views, which expose a *single domain's* dimensional model through the semantic layer — grain-defended, SCD2, conformed — so an analyst can drill and slice within Trading without a physical star schema to trip over. Both are real and both are covered. Neither is the thing a business user or a data scientist actually wants when they are exploring, which is to pivot freely across the *whole* business — to start in revenue, swing into trading volume, land on counterparty risk, and finish in custody positions, in one unbroken line of thought, without anyone having pre-built the join.

That capability has a name in this architecture, and it is the consumption face of the colimit. The glued ontology — the domains and the bridges between them — *is* a dimensional model that spans the enterprise. You do not materialise it as a giant star; you navigate it, live, Silver-direct, through the semantic layer. This chapter is about what that navigation is, how it works mechanically, and the one genuinely hard problem it raises.

=== One Exploratory Session, Many Domains

Picture the session, not the query. A risk analyst starts in Risk: counterparty exposure, drawn from the Risk domain's Silver dimensional model exposed as a governed view. She drills by counterparty. She wants instrument detail — that lives in Trading — so she pivots, and the semantic layer composes the Trading dimensions onto her current view through the Trading–Risk bridge, resolving that "exposure" means something different on each side and declaring which it is using. She pivots again to custody region; the Custody bridge carries her there. Three domains, one session, no pre-built mart, every hop governed.

Mechanically, this is the colimit doing exactly what Chapter 4 said it does, turned into a consumption surface:

* *Each domain contributes its dimensional model Silver-direct.* The drill-and-slice power comes from Silver — grain, conformed concepts, history-where-tiered — exposed as a governed view, never a physical star in Gold. The analyst sees a logical dimensional surface; the semantic layer generates the SQL.
* *Bridges compose the surfaces.* A pivot across a domain boundary loads the bridge between the two domains (the same span-through-a-shared-sub-theory from "Multi-Domain Composition") and joins on the shared concept — at the right time grain, via the As-Of Join rule from the Time chapter, because cross-domain joins are exactly where time-between-entities bites.
* *Register-switching resolves polysemy as she crosses.* "Position," "exposure," "volume" mean different things per domain; the semantic layer holds each domain's definition and declares which one the composed view is using, so the pivot does not silently change what a word means.
* *The view is the colimit, scoped to the session.* She is never querying "the whole enterprise model." She is querying the sub-colimit of the domains she has actually reached — the local manifold of her question — which is why this stays answerable as the estate grows.

// TODO: figure asset — cross-domain dimensional navigation: three domain dimensional models, bridges as the composing morphisms, one analyst path traversing them, with the access-policy intersection shaded.
[#fig-cross-ontology-dimensional]
.Exploring the Glued Ontology — domain dimensional models composed Silver-direct through bridges into one navigable surface
image::figures/cross-ontology-dimensional.png[Cross-Ontology Dimensional Exploration,width=720,align=center]

=== Whose Security Governs the Joined Cell?

Here is the hard problem, and it deserves to be named rather than waved at, because it is the one that clean rooms exist to solve. When a Trading row and a Compliance row are composed across a bridge, *whose* access policy governs the resulting cell? The analyst may be cleared for Trading and not for the Compliance attributes that the bridge would pull in. If composition silently widens her view, the architecture has built a privilege-escalation engine; if it silently drops the restricted columns, she gets a partial answer that looks complete.

The rule is that **composed access is the intersection, declared at the bridge, never inferred at query time.** A cross-domain view exposes a cell only if the consumer is authorised for it in *every* domain that contributed to it; where she is not, the cell is withheld *visibly* — a typed "restricted," not a silent omission — and the view tells her a Compliance grant is what would complete it. The bridge is where the row- and column-level policy of the two domains is composed and recorded, the same way the bridge records what maps and what is lost. This is the access-control face of the sharing rule: you cannot compose what the consumer cannot see in both places, and the system says so out loud rather than guessing.

*Pattern: Cross-Ontology Explore*

_Context:_ A business or data-science user explores across domain boundaries in one session — pivoting through dimensions that live in different domains — rather than asking a single pre-anticipated question.

_Problem:_ Flat Gold answers only its built-for question; single-domain Silver-as-Gold drills only within one domain. The cross-domain exploratory pivot either becomes a bespoke mart (rigid, one question, weeks of engineering) or a hand-written join (ungoverned, polysemy-blind, security-blind).

_Solution:_ Expose the colimit as a live navigable surface. Each domain contributes its Silver dimensional model as a governed view; bridges compose the surfaces on pivot, joining on shared concepts via as-of alignment; register-switching declares which domain's definition each polysemous term carries; access is the intersection of contributing domains' policies, composed at the bridge and withheld visibly where absent. The user navigates the sub-colimit of the domains she reaches — no pre-built mart, every hop governed.

_Example:_ Exposure (Risk) → instrument (Trading) → custody region (Custody), one session. Each pivot loads the relevant bridge, resolves the polysemous term ("exposure": Risk's PFE-inclusive vs Trading's mark-to-market), as-of-joins on the shared counterparty identity, and exposes only cells the analyst is cleared for in all three domains; the Compliance KYC attribute she lacks rights to shows as `restricted`, with the grant that would unlock it named.

_Consequences:_ Live cross-domain composition is slower than a pre-materialised mart and leans hard on bridge quality — a missing or stale bridge surfaces as a gap the analyst hits mid-pivot, not a wrong number, which is the right failure but still a failure. For the highest-traffic cross-domain questions, materialise the composed view as a governed flat Gold table (the colimit told you exactly how to build it); keep the live explore for the long tail of questions nobody anticipated.

=== Who This Is For

Two consumers, the same surface. The **business user** gets genuine self-serve: not "here are the ten dashboards we pre-built," but "pivot anywhere the bridges reach, and where they do not, the gap is named and becomes the next morphism to write." The **data scientist** gets cross-domain *feature discovery* — the ability to find and assemble features that span domains (settlement behaviour from Custody against trading patterns from Trading against KYC recency from Compliance) without first standing up a join nobody will govern. Both are doing the same thing: walking the glued ontology. The difference between an organisation that can do this and one that cannot is not the number of Gold tables. It is whether the bridges exist — the connective tissue from "Multi-Domain Composition," now earning its keep as the thing that makes the next question free.

== Time

Time is one modelling discipline among many, and the fastest way to wreck a data platform is to treat it as the whole game. Done in fear — full historical machinery on every table, two time axes on every row — it produces models nobody can query and costs nobody can justify. Done in denial — overwrite-in-place, one timestamp, hope — it produces a warehouse that cannot answer the one question an auditor always asks. The discipline is to treat time *carefully where it matters and simply where it does not*, and to know the difference before you model, not after the inquiry.

There is one temporal distinction worth building the chapter around, because it is the one that turns audits into arguments when it is missed. The question "what was our P&L last quarter?" has two structurally different answers. One reconstructs *what was true* — applying every correction, reclassification, and late-arriving fact available today. The other reconstructs *what was reported* — what the system said at the time, with whatever it knew on the day the report was pulled. Both are real, both legitimate, and most architectures conflate them by omission: the infrastructure to keep them distinguishable was never built, because nobody asked until the worst possible moment — a regulatory inquiry, a restatement, or an agent that inherits a baked-in temporal assumption and confidently answers the wrong one of the two.

That distinction is worth getting right. It does not follow that you pay for it everywhere. Most of this chapter is about *where* the heavy machinery earns its cost, where a single timestamp is plenty, and how to handle the cases the bitemporal literature is silent on — joining entities that keep time differently, data arriving as a stream, a feed whose cadence changes under you, and the awkward collision between "we never delete" and a regulator who can compel deletion. Bitemporality is a tool in this chapter, not its thesis.

=== The Two Questions

At any moment, somebody will ask what happened on a particular date. Auditor, regulator, analyst, risk model, LLM agent composing a quarterly summary —- the identity varies, but the question is always one of two structurally different questions, and the architecture must answer both while keeping them distinguishable.


[#fig-two-questions]
.Two questions, one fact: what was ‘true’ versus what was ‘reported.’ The architecture must answer both and keep them distinct.
image::figures/two-questions.png[two questions,width=600,align="center"]

First: what was true? What is now known about the state of the world on that date, with late-arriving facts received, corrections applied, reclassifications processed. A counterparty that was labelled "retail" on the day of the trade but was reclassified to "institutional" three weeks later now appears under its corrected classification. A revenue figure that was booked provisionally at month-end and adjusted during the close cycle now reflects the adjusted amount. Current best reconstruction of the past, incorporating everything learned since. Sometimes called valid time — the time at which things were true in the world, as best understood today.

Second: what was reported? What did the system say at the time? Reports produced on day T used the data the warehouse held on day T —- data since corrected, augmented, and reclassified. Those reports are themselves part of the historical record: what regulators received, what management acted on, what the board reviewed. The number the warehouse now believes correct and the number it published three weeks ago are both real, both legitimate, and frequently different. Sometimes called system time or transaction time — when the warehouse recorded or reported a fact.

[#fig-two-clocks]
.One Fact, Two Clocks — the same fact plotted on valid time (x) and system time (y); a correction moves it back and up, and the old fact is never overwritten
image::figures/two-clocks.png[One Fact, Two Clocks,width=720,align=center]


Both correct. Different questions, different consumers, different needs. Risk management needs "what was true" — the current best estimate of exposure, incorporating every correction. Regulatory submissions need "what was reported" — the filing captured reality as it was known, and retroactively changing filed numbers creates its own regulatory problems. Finance needs both, at different moments in the close cycle. Treasury needs both, for different purposes: mark-to-market versus reported position. An architecture that cannot serve both forces consumers to work around it, and workarounds in financial data are how control breakdowns begin.

A trade is booked on March 15. The counterparty is classified as "retail" based on the KYC records current at booking time. The trade flows through the nightly pipeline, lands in Silver, gets picked up by the daily P&L Gold table, and appears in the March 31 quarter-end report with a retail counterparty classification. Everything is clean.

On April 2, a KYC review reclassifies the counterparty from "retail" to "institutional." The reclassification is backdated to March 10 — the counterparty should have been classified as institutional before the trade was even booked. The correction enters the system on April 2, but its valid-time effect reaches back to March 10.


Now pull the P&L report on April 15. The numbers are different. The counterparty is institutional, not retail. The segmentation buckets have shifted. Aggregates that roll up by customer segment show different totals. If the institutional tier carries different fee structures or risk weightings, the P&L itself may have changed. If the institution uses different capital-adequacy treatments for institutional versus retail counterparties, the downstream capital calculations have shifted as well — and those calculations may have been submitted to a regulator in the interim.

Which report is right? Both. March 31 correctly represents what the system knew on March 31. April 15 correctly represents what the system now knows about the same period. A regulator asking "what did you report at quarter-end?" needs the March 31 version. A risk manager asking "what is our actual exposure by counterparty segment?" needs the April 15 version. An agent that does not know which question it is answering will confidently return one when the consumer meant the other, and the confidence is what makes the error dangerous — a hesitant wrong answer invites scrutiny; a confident one gets acted on.
